The Philosophers' Inconsistency and Limitation of God's Knowledge
Ghazali presents an objection and a two-part response regarding God's knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali exposes internal contradictions among philosophers and argues that even if God's knowledge causes emanation, it only covers the first effect.

Core Idea
Ghazali turns the philosophers' own system against them: knowledge only reaches the direct effect.

In this passage, Ghazali presents a two-pronged refutation of the philosophers' claim that God's knowledge of the universal order is necessary for creation. First, he shows inconsistency among philosophers: some deny that knowledge is the cause, insisting on brute necessary emanation. Second, he argues that even on their own emanation scheme, God only directly produces the first intellect, so His knowledge would be limited to that. This argument is powerful because it uses the philosophers' own premises against them.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and Ghazali's response
Read the full Arabic passage and its translation carefully to grasp the flow: objection, then Ghazali's two points.
Notice the structure: 'If it is said... We say...' then 'the second aspect...'
2
Identify the two parts of Ghazali's response
Notice how Ghazali first points out that other philosophers disagree with the objector, then presents a second argument that limits God's knowledge.
The first part addresses inconsistency; the second part is a direct argument about the scope of knowledge.
3
Compare Ghazali's two arguments
Contrast the strategies: one shows inconsistency among philosophers, the other shows internal limitation within their own scheme.
4
Reflect on the example of the stone
How does the stone example illustrate that even in voluntary action, knowledge of the direct cause does not entail knowledge of all effects? This is key to Ghazali's second point.
Apply the stone example to the emanation scheme: God knows only the first effect, not the subsequent chain.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : بين الأمرين فرق ، وهو أن صدور الكل عن علمه بالكل ، فتمثل النظام الكلى فتمثل النظام الكلى ، هو سبب فيضان الكل ، ولا سبب له سوى العلم بالكل ، والعلم بالكل عين ذاته ، فلو لم يكن له علم بالكل لما وجد منه الكل ، بخلاف النور من الشمس .
قلنا : وفى هذا خالفك إخوانك ، فإنهم قالوا : ذاته تعالى ذات ، يلزم منها وجود الكل ، على ترتيبه بالطبع والاضطرار ، لا من حيث إنه عالم به ، فها المحيل لهذا المذهب ، مهما وافقتهم على نفى الإرادة ؟! وكما لم يشترط علم الشمس بالنور ، للزوم النور ، بل يتبعها النور ضرورة ، فليقدر ذلك في الأول ولا مانع منه .
سلم لهم
أنه الوجه الثاني : هو أن صدور الشيء من الفاعل ، يقتضى العلم أيضاً بالصادر ، فعندهم فعل الله تعالى واحد ، وهو المعلول الأول ، الذي هو عقل بسيط ، فينبغى ألا يكون عالماً إلا به ، والمعلول الأول يكون عالماً أيضاً ، بما صدر منه فقط ، فإن الكل لم يوجد من الله تعالى دفعة واحدة ، بل بالوساطة والتولد واللزوم ، والذي يصدر ممن يصدر منه ، لم ينبغي أن يكون معلوماً له ؟! ولم يصدر منه إلا شيء واحد ؟ بل هذا لا يلزم في الفعل الإدارى، فكيف فى الطبيعى ؟ ! ، فإن حركة الحجر من فوق جبل، قد تكون بتحريك إرادى ، يوجب العلم بأصل الحركة ، ولا يوجب العلم بما تتعلد منها بوساطتها، من مصادمته ، وكسره غيره ، فهذا أيضاً لا جواب لهم عنه
If it is said: 'There is a difference between the two matters, which is that the emanation of the whole is from His knowledge of the whole, so the representation of the universal order is the cause of the overflow of the whole, and there is no cause for it except knowledge of the whole, and knowledge of the whole is identical to His essence. So if He had no knowledge of the whole, the whole would not come forth from Him, unlike the light from the sun.' We say: 'In this, your brethren differ from you, for they said: His essence, exalted, is an essence from which the existence of the whole necessarily follows in its order by nature and necessity, not by virtue of His being knowledgeable of it. So what is the obstacle to this doctrine when you agree with them in denying will?! And just as the sun's knowledge of light is not required for the necessity of light – rather, light follows it necessarily – let that be assumed in the First, and there is no obstacle.' [Then] the second aspect: is that the emanation of a thing from an agent also necessitates knowledge of what emanates. In their view, God's action is one, namely the first effect, which is a simple intellect, so He should not be knowledgeable except of it. And the first effect is also knowledgeable only of what emanates from it. For the whole did not come from God all at once, but through mediation, generation, and necessary entailment. And that which emanates from the one from whom it emanates should not be known to him?! And nothing emanates from Him except one thing? Rather, this is not necessary in voluntary action, so how about natural action? For the movement of a stone from a mountain may be by voluntary movement which necessitates knowledge of the origin of the movement, but does not necessitate knowledge of what is generated from it through mediation, such as its collision and breaking something else. So this too, they have no answer for it.
Source pages p. 201

Commentary
This passage is an objection and Ghazali's response (the second part of his overall answer). The objection argues that there is a difference between God's action and natural actions like the sun: in God's case, the emanation of the universe is caused by His knowledge of the universal order, and that knowledge is identical to His essence. So if He lacked that knowledge, the universe would not exist. This is unlike the sun, which does not need knowledge to emit light. Thus, God's knowledge is necessary for creation, and so He must know all details. Ghazali's response has two parts. First, he points out that other philosophers (referred to as 'your brethren') actually hold that God's essence necessitates the universe by nature and necessity, not by knowledge. So the objector is inconsistent: if he agrees with them in denying will, why reject their view that knowledge is not the cause? And just as the sun's knowledge is not required for light, it can be assumed similarly for God. Second, he argues that even if we accept that knowledge causes emanation, according to the philosophers' own emanation scheme, God directly brings about only the first effect (the first intellect), which is a simple intellect. Therefore, God should only know that first effect, not all subsequent effects that arise through mediation and generation. He illustrates with an example: a stone moved voluntarily from a mountain – the mover knows the origin of the motion but may not know all the accidental consequences (collisions, breaking). Similarly, God's knowledge would only extend to His direct act, not the chain of effects. This, Ghazali asserts, is an argument the philosophers cannot answer.

Key Terms
النظام الكلى (universal order)
فيضان (overflow/emanation)
المعلول الأول (first effect)
عقل بسيط (simple intellect)
بالوساطة والتولد (by mediation and generation)
اللزوم (necessary entailment)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if God's knowledge causes the world, He must know everything, but Ghazali shows that even in that scheme, He would only know the immediate effect.
Correction: Ghazali argues that according to the philosophers' own emanation scheme, knowledge only extends to the direct cause, not the entire chain of effects.

Checkpoint
What does Ghazali argue about the philosophers' claim that God's knowledge of the whole causes its emanation?
A) He accepts it but says it does not affect the argument.
B) He shows that other philosophers deny knowledge as cause, and even if it is cause, God only knows the first effect.
C) He argues that this makes God's action voluntary.
D) He says it is the same as the sun's action.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali's argument shows that the philosophers' system either lacks consistency or limits God's knowledge to only the first effect, undermining their claim of exhaustive divine knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you imagine God's knowledge, do you picture it as a detailed plan that leaves nothing to chance? How does Ghazali's argument—that even the philosophers' own system cannot support exhaustive knowledge—expose a potential arrogance in assuming you can fully comprehend God's knowledge? Will you commit to accepting that God's knowledge is beyond your grasp, even if philosophers claim to define its limits?
?
Consider the stone moved from the mountain: the mover knows only the origin, not the collisions. Do you ever assume that because you plan an action, you control all its consequences? How might your humility before God deepen if you acknowledge that even your voluntary actions produce effects you cannot foresee, while God alone knows every ripple? Would you be willing to repent of the pride that thinks you know where your actions lead?
?
Ghazali conceded—for argument's sake—that God's essence could necessitate the universe without knowledge. Does your heart find rest in a God who acts by necessary nature, or in a God who freely wills and knows every detail? Which vision makes you more accountable in your worship? Will you examine whether you secretly prefer a God who has no choice because that absolves you of personal response?

The Self-Refuting Nature of the Philosophers' Claim
Ghazali refutes the impossibility of the first temporal event from the eternal using the philosophers' own circular motion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's argument that the philosophers' own principles allow the eternal to produce temporal events, thus they cannot consistently deny the first temporal event from the eternal.

Core Idea
The impossibility of the first event is self-refuting under the philosophers' own system.

In this passage, Ghazali turns the philosophers' own argument against them. He shows that they admit temporal events emanate from the eternal through circular motion, so the condition for impossibility must be the event being first, not temporal. This leads to a powerful refutation that also applies to divine knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify Ghazali's main claim.
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice how Ghazali begins by stating that each division is not impossible according to their own principles.
The passage is dense; focus on the logical flow.
2
Understand the condition for impossibility.
Ghazali points out that the philosophers' impossibility is conditional on the event being the first, not on being temporal per se. Mark the phrase 'شرط استحالته كونه أولا'.
This is the key to the refutation.
3
Trace the argument using circular motion.
Ghazali reminds the philosophers that they already accept temporal events emanating from the eternal via the celestial soul and circular motion. Follow how he connects this to uniform states and perpetual emanation.
Compare with the conclusion about temporal knowledges.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قلنا : كل واحد من القسمين غير محال على أصلكم . أما قولكم : إنه يستحيل أن يصدر ، من القديم حادث هو أول الحوادث ، فقد أبطلناه فى تلك المسألة ، كيف ؟ ! وعندكم يستحيل يصدر من القديم حادث ، هو أول الحوادث ، فشرط استحالته ، كونه أولا ، وإلا فهذه الحوادث ، ليست لها أسباب حادثة إلى غير نهاية ، بل تنتهى بوساطة الحركة الدورية ، إلى شيء قديم ، هو نفس الفلك وحياته ، فالنفس الفلكية قديمة ، والحركة الدورية تحدث منها ، وكل جزء من أجزاء الحركة ، يحدث ، وينقضى ، وما بعده متجدد لا محالة ، فإذن الحوادث صادرة من القديم عندكم ، ولكن إذا تشابهت أحوال القديم ، تشابه فيضان الحوادث منه على الدوام ، كما تشابهت أحوال الحركة ، لما أن كانت تصدر من قديم متشابه الأحوال . فاستبان أن كل فريق مبهم ، معترف بأنه يجوز صدور الحوادث من قديم، إذا كانت تصدر على التناسب والدوام ، فلتكن العلوم الحادثة من هذا القبيل
We say: Each of the two divisions is not impossible according to your principle. As for your statement that it is impossible for a temporal event which is the first of events to emanate from the Eternal, we have already refuted that in that issue. How?! According to you, it is impossible for a temporal event which is the first of events to emanate from the Eternal; so the condition of its impossibility is its being first. Otherwise, these temporal events have no temporal causes ad infinitum, but terminate through the intermediary of circular motion to something eternal, which is the heaven itself and its life. For the celestial soul is eternal, and circular motion occurs from it, and every part of the parts of motion occurs and passes away, and what follows it is necessarily renewed. So temporal events emanate from the Eternal according to you. But if the states of the Eternal are uniform, then the emanation of events from it is uniform perpetually, just as the states of motion are uniform, since they emanate from an eternal whose states are uniform. Thus it becomes clear that every vague party acknowledges that it is possible for events to emanate from an Eternal, if they emanate proportionally and perpetually. So let the temporal knowledges be of this kind.
Source pages p. 216

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the philosophers' claim that the Eternal cannot be the source of the first temporal event. Ghazali begins by stating that each of the two divisions (presumably the two possible scenarios he is discussing) is not impossible according to the philosophers' own principles. He then addresses their specific claim that it is impossible for the Eternal to produce a temporal event that is the first of events. He says he has already refuted that in an earlier discussion. He then argues: according to the philosophers, the impossibility is conditional on the event being the first; otherwise, if there is an infinite chain of events, they terminate through the intermediary of circular motion to something eternal — the heaven and its life. The celestial soul is eternal, and from it arise circular motions. Each part of motion occurs and passes away, and the next is constantly renewed. Thus, according to their own system, events do emanate from the Eternal, but they do so perpetually and uniformly because the Eternal's states are uniform. He concludes that they all admit the possibility of events from an Eternal if they are proportional and perpetual, so temporal knowledges (the knowledge of events) can also be of this kind.

Key Terms
قديم
حادث
حركة دورية
فلك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The philosophers believe that no temporal event can emanate from the Eternal at all.
Correction: Ghazali shows that they themselves admit temporal events emanate from the Eternal via circular motion; they only deny the first event.

Checkpoint
What is the condition for the impossibility of the Eternal producing the first temporal event according to the philosophers?
A) That the event is temporal.
B) That the event is the first event.
C) That the event is knowledge.
D) That the event is infinite.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali shows that the philosophers cannot consistently deny the first temporal event from the eternal because they already admit temporal events emanate from the eternal via circular motion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Ghazali shows that the philosophers' own system forces them to admit that temporal events can come from the Eternal. In your own life, do you sometimes deny God's involvement in the 'first' or most foundational events (like the beginning of a new relationship, career, or crisis) while acknowledging His hand in the ongoing? How would your perspective change if you truly believed that every first event also comes from God?
?
The passage concludes that 'temporal knowledges' (i.e., God's knowledge of temporal events) can also be of this kind — emanating from the Eternal. When you face a situation that feels entirely new or unprecedented, do you trust that God's knowledge of it is eternal and perfect? What would it look like to live with the conviction that nothing is truly 'first' for God?
?
The philosophers' mistake was to impose a condition (being first) that their own system contradicts. Are there conditions or reservations you place on God's power or knowledge — secretly thinking 'God can handle this, but not that' or 'God knows this, but not that'? How can you remove those mental conditions and submit to the absolute perfection of God's attributes?

Three Matters Regarding Temporal Events as Cause for Divine Knowledge
Ghazali enumerates and refutes three objections to temporal events causing knowledge in the First.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali refutes three philosophical objections to temporal events causing knowledge in the First, using their own principles.

Core Idea
Ghazali turns the philosophers' own premises against them to defend temporal events as a cause of divine knowledge.

In this passage, Ghazali addresses the second division of his argument: the possibility that knowledge arises in the First from another (temporal events). He enumerates three objections the philosophers might raise and systematically dismantles each one by drawing on their own accepted principles. This lesson walks through each of those three matters and Ghazali's rebuttals.

Guided Path
1
Understand the context
This passage is part of Ghazali's discussion on divine knowledge. He has already argued that if knowledge arises from the essence, it leads to multiplicity. Now he considers the alternative: that knowledge comes from temporal events.
This is the second division (القسم الثانى) of his argument.
2
Identify the three matters
Ghazali lists three concerns: change (تغير), another being a cause (كون الغير سبباً), and the Eternal being changed by another (كون القديم متغيراً بغيره). Locate each one in the passage.
The third matter also involves the idea of subjugation (تسخير).
3
Examine the first rebuttal: change
Ghazali argues that the philosophers already accept changing eternals (like celestial souls), so change is not impossible for them. Note how he references his earlier demonstration of necessity.
He says 'قد بينا لزومه على أصلكم' (we have shown its necessity according to your principle).
4
Examine the second rebuttal: vision analogy
Ghazali compares the process to vision: a colored object causes an impression in the eye through transparent air. Similarly, a temporal event can cause knowledge in the First, which is prepared to receive it from potency to act.
This analogy uses their own accepted causal principles.
5
Examine the third rebuttal: subjugation
Ghazali points out that their own system already implies subjugation because they hold that God acts by necessity and nature without choice. So they cannot object to this characterization.
He says even if it resembles subjugation, it is consistent with their own views.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما القسم الثانى ، وهو صدور هذا العلم فيه من غيره ، فنقول : ولم يستحيل ذلك عندكم ؟ ! وليس فيه إلا ثلاثة أمور : أحدها : التغير ، قد بينا لزومه على أصلكم والثاني : كون الغير سبباً لتغير الغير ، وهو ليس بمحال عندكم ، فليكن حدوث الشيء سبباً ، لحدوث العلم به ؛ كما أنكم تقولون : تمثل الشخص المتلون ، بإزاء الحدقة الباصرة ، سبب لانطباع مثال الشخص ، في الطبقة الجليدية من الحدقة ، عند توسط الهواء المشف ، بين الحدقة والمبصر . فإذا جاز أن يكون ، جماد سبباً لانطباع الصورة في الحدقة ، وهو معنى الإبصار ، فلم يستحيل أن يكون ، حدوث الحوادث ، سبباً لحصول علم الأول بها ، فإن القوة الباصرة ، كما أنها مستعدة للإدراك ، ويكون حصول الشخص المتلون ، مع ارتفاع الحواجز ، سبباً لحصول الإدراك ، فلتكن ذات المبدأ الأول ، عندكم مستعدة لقبول العلم ، ويخرج من القوة إلى الفعل ، بوجود ذلك الحادث ، فإن كان فيه تغير القديم، فالقديم المتغير عندكم غير مستحيل . وإن زعمتم أن ذلك يستحيل في واجب الوجود ، فليس لكم على إثبات واجب الوجود ، دليل" ، إلا قطع سلسلة العلل والمعلولات ، كما سبق ، وقد بينا أن قطع التسلسل ممكن ، بقديم متغير والأمر الثالث : الذي يتضمنه هذا (۱) ، هو كون القديم متغيراً بغيره (۲) وأن ذلك يشبه التسخير ، واستيلاء الغير عليه ، فيقال : ولم يستحيل عندكم هذا ؟ وهو أن يكون هو سبباً لحدوث الحوادث بوسائط ، ثم يكون حدوث الحوادث ، سبباً لحصول العلم له بها ، فكأنه هو السبب في تحصيل العلم لنفسه ، ولكن بالوسائط وقولكم : إن ذلك يشبه التسخير ، فليكن كذلك ، فإنه لائق بأصلكم أن ما إذ زعمتم يصدر من الله تعالى إنما يصدر على سبيل اللزوم والطبع ولا قدرة له على أن لا يفعل ، وهذا أيضاً يشبه نوعاً من التسخير ، ويشير إلى أنه كالمضطر ، في ما يصدر منه
As for the second division — namely, the occurrence of this knowledge in Him from another — we say: Why is that impossible according to you? There are only three matters in it: The first: change (taghayyur). We have already shown its necessity according to your principle. The second: that another is a cause for the change of another. That is not impossible according to you. So let the occurrence of a thing be a cause for the occurrence of knowledge of it, just as you say: The presentation of a colored object opposite the seeing pupil is a cause for the impression of the image of the object in the ice-like layer of the pupil, when transparent air mediates between the pupil and the seen object. So if it is possible that an inanimate object be a cause for the impression of the form in the pupil — which is the meaning of sight — then why is it impossible that the occurrence of events be a cause for obtaining knowledge of them by the First? For the visual faculty is, just as it is prepared for perception, and the presence of the colored object with the removal of barriers is a cause for obtaining perception, so let the essence of the First Principle be, according to you, prepared to accept knowledge, and it may go from potency to act by the existence of that event. If this involves change in the Eternal, then the changing Eternal is not impossible according to you. And if you claim that that is impossible for the Necessary Existent, then you have no proof for establishing the Necessary Existent except the cutting of the chain of causes and effects, as previously mentioned, and we have shown that the cutting of the chain is possible by a changing eternal. And the third matter: that which it contains, namely that the Eternal is changed by another, and that resembles subjugation (taskhir) and the domination of another over Him. So it is said: Why is that impossible according to you? That He Himself is a cause for the occurrence of events through intermediaries, and then the occurrence of events is a cause for obtaining knowledge of them for Him, as if He is the cause for acquiring knowledge for Himself, but through intermediaries. And your statement that this resembles subjugation: let it be so, for it is fitting with your principle that what you claim emanates from Allah, may He be exalted, emanates only in the manner of necessity and nature, and He has no power to not do it. This also resembles a kind of subjugation and indicates that He is like compelled with respect to what emanates from Him.
Source pages p. 216

Commentary
This passage deals with the second division: the possibility that knowledge in the First arises from another (i.e., from temporal events). Ghazali first asks rhetorically why the philosophers consider this impossible. He then enumerates three concerns: (1) change (taghayyur), (2) the other being a cause of change for another, and (3) the Eternal being changed by another, which resembles subjugation. For each, he provides a rebuttal drawing on the philosophers' own principles. On change, he notes they already have changing eternals (like celestial souls). On the second, he uses an analogy from vision: just as a colored object causes an impression in the eye, so a temporal event can cause knowledge in the First; the First is prepared to receive knowledge, and the event brings it from potency to act. He argues that if this involves change in the Eternal, they have no grounds to deem it impossible, especially since their proof for the Necessary Existent rests only on cutting the chain of causes, which could be achieved by a changing eternal. On the third matter, he points out that even if it resembles subjugation, their own system already implies subjugation because God acts by necessity and nature without choice. So they cannot object to this characterization.

Key Terms
تغير
سبب
تسخير
قديم متغير
قوة وفعل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think Ghazali is affirming that God actually changes. In fact, he is using the philosophers' own premises to show they cannot rule out change.
Correction: Ghazali is employing a dialectical argument, not asserting his own view. He demonstrates that if they accept changing eternals and causal relations, they cannot deny the possibility of temporal events causing divine knowledge.

Checkpoint
What analogy does Ghazali use to argue that a temporal event can cause knowledge in the First?
A) The reflection of the sun on water.
B) The impression of an image on the eye.
C) The movement of the celestial spheres.
D) The creation of the world from nothing.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali demonstrates that the philosophers' own principles do not allow them to reject the possibility that temporal events cause divine knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Ghazali uses the analogy of vision to show that temporal events can be a cause for knowledge in God. When you look at the events of your own life—both good and bad—do you see them as mere happenings or as opportunities that actualize something in your relationship with God? How can you start to see each moment as a cause for drawing closer to Him?
?
The philosophers objected that temporal causation would imply subjugation of God, but Ghazali turned the argument, showing their own view entails compulsion. Is there an area of your faith where you hold a standard for God that you would not accept for yourself? What would it take to align your expectations with the humility this lesson demands?
?
This passage shows the power of using your opponent's premises against them. Identify one assumption you hold about God or His actions that may actually be borrowed from a worldview inconsistent with tawhid. Are you willing to examine that assumption and possibly let it go?

The Philosophers' Proof for the Heavens Being Alive and Its Critique
Ghazali presents and critiques the philosophers' proof by elimination.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' rational proof that the heavens are animate beings and Ghazali's critique that such reasoning only produces probable opinion, not certain knowledge.

Core Idea
Rational proofs can yield opinion but not certainty about metaphysical claims like the heavens' life.

The philosophers argue that the heavens must be alive because their motion is volitional. Ghazali presents their proof by elimination but immediately cautions that such reasoning, while possible, does not amount to certainty. In this lesson, we will unpack both the proof and its limitations.

Guided Path
1
Understand the philosophers' claim
Read the opening passage where Ghazali states the philosophers' view: the heavens have souls, move by will, and worship God through their rotational motion.
Note that Ghazali does not deny the possibility; he only denies rational certainty.
2
Identify the three types of motion
The philosophers divide motion into compulsory, natural, and volitional. Find this classification in the Arabic text and understand each type.
These three categories are exhaustive according to the philosophers.
3
Trace the elimination argument
Follow how the philosophers eliminate compulsory and natural motion, leaving volitional as the only option for the heavens' motion.
The elimination of compulsory motion involves an infinite regress argument.
4
Grasp Ghazali's critique
Ghazali asserts that this entire proof only yields ظن (opinion), not قطع (certainty). Reflect on why he thinks rational analogy is insufficient here.
Ghazali suggests such knowledge, if true, comes only from prophecy.

مسألة [١٤] في تعجيزهم عن إقامة الدليل على أن السماء حيوان مطيع الله تعالى بحركته الدورية. وقد قالوا: إن السماء حيوان، وإن لها نفساً، نسبتها إلى بدن السماء، كنسبة نفوسنا إلى أبداننا، وكما أن أبداننا تتحرك بالإرادة، نحو أغراضها، بتحريك النفس، فكذا السموات، وإن غرض السموات بحركتها الدورية، عبادة رب العالمين، على وجه سنذكره. ومذهبهم في هذه المسألة، مما لا ينكر إمكانه، ولا يدعى استحالته، فإن الله تعالى قادر على أن يخلق الحياة في كل جسم، فلا كبر الجسم يمنع من كونه حيا، ولا كونه مستديراً، فإن الشكل المخصوص، ليس شرطاً للحياة، إذ الحيوانات، اختلاف أشكالها، مشتركة في قبول الحياة. ولكنا ندعى عجزهم، عن معرفة ذلك بدليل العقل، فإن هذا إن كان صحيحاً، فلا يطلع عليه إلا الأنبياء - صلوات الله عليهم - بإلهام من الله تعالى، أو بوحى، وقياس العقل ليس يدل عليه. نعم لا يبعد أن يعرف مثل ذلك بدليل، إن وجد الدليل وساعد. نقولى: ما أوردوه دليلا، لا يصلح إلا لإفادة ظن، فأما أن يفيد قطعاً فلا. وخبالهم فيه أن قالوا: إن السماء متحركة – وهذه مقدمة حسية – وكل جسم متحرك، فله محرك وهذه مقدمة عقلية – إذ لو كان الجسم يتحرك لكونه جسما، لكان كل جسم متحركاً. وكل محرك، فإما أن يكون منبعثاً، عن ذات المتحرك، كالطبيعة في حركة الحجر إلى أسفل، والإرادة في حركة الحيوان القدرة، وإما أن يكون المحرك خارجاً، ولكن يحرك عن طريق القسر، كدفع الحجر إلى فوق. وكل ما يتحرك بمعنى في ذاته، فإما أن لا يشعر ذلك الشيء ونحن نسميه طبيعة كحركة الحجر إلى أسفل، وإما أن يشعر بها، ونحن نسميه إرادياً ونفسانيا. فصارت الحركة بهذه التقسمات الحاضرة، الدائرة بين النفى والإثبات، إما قسرية وإما طبيعية، وإما إرادية، وإذا بطل قسمان تعين الثالث. ولا يمكن أن يكون قسريا، لأن المحرك القاسر، إما جسم أو جسم آخر، يتحرك بالإرادة أو بالقسر، وينتهى لا محالة، إلى إرادة، ومهما ثبت في أجسام السموات، متحرك بالإرادة، فقد حصل الغرض؛ فأى فائدة في وضع حركات قسرية، وبالآخرة لابد من الرجوع إلى الإرادة؟ وإما أن يقال إنه يتحرك بالقسر، والله تعالى هو المحرك بغير واسطة، وهو محال، لأنه لو تحرك به من حيث إنه جسم، وإنه خالقه، للزم أن يتحرك كل جسم، فلابد أن تختص الحركة بصفة، بها يتميز عن غيره من الأجسام وتلك الصفة هي المحرك القريب، فإما الإرادة أو الطبع، ولا يمكن أن يقال: إن الله تعالى يحركه بالإرادة، لأن إرادته تناسب الأجسام نسبة واحدة، فلم استعد هذا الجسم على الخصوص، لأن يراد تحريكه دون غيره، ولا يمكن أن يكون ذلك جزافاً، فإن ذلك محال، كما سبق في مسألة حدوث العالم. وإذا ثبت أن هذا الجسم، ينبغى أن يكون فيه صفة هي مبدأ الحركة. بطل القسم الأول، وهو تقدير الحركة القسرية. فيبقى أن يقال: هي طبيعية، وهو غير ممكن، لأن الطبيعة بمجردها، قطعاً لا تكون سبباً للحركة، لأن معنى الحركة، هرب من مكان، وطلب لمكان آخر، فالمكان الذي فيه الجسم، إن كان ملائماً له، فلا يتحرك عنه؛ ولهذا لا يتحرك زق مملوء من الهواء، على وجه الماء، إلى أسفل، وإذا غمس في الماء، تحرك إلى وجه الماء، لأنه وجد المكان الملائم، فسكن، والطبيعة معه
Issue [14] on showing their incapacity to establish proof that the heaven is an animal obedient to God Most High by its rotational motion. And they said: the heaven is an animal, and it has a soul, its relation to the body of heaven is like the relation of our souls to our bodies, and just as our bodies move by will towards their purposes by the movement of the soul, so are the heavens, and the purpose of the heavens in their rotational motion is worship of the Lord of the worlds, in a manner we will mention. And their doctrine in this issue is not such that its possibility is denied nor its impossibility claimed, for God Most High is able to create life in every body, so neither the largeness of the body prevents it from being alive, nor its being spherical, for the specific shape is not a condition for life, since animals, diverse in their shapes, share in receiving life. But we claim their incapacity to know that by rational proof, for if this is true, it is not known except by the prophets - prayers of God upon them - through inspiration from God Most High, or by revelation, and rational analogy does not indicate it. Yes, it is not unlikely that such a thing be known by a proof, if the proof exists and assists. We say: what they have adduced as a proof is only suitable for producing opinion, but as for producing certainty, no. And their fallacy in it is that they said: The heaven is moving - and this is a sensory premise - and every moving body has a mover - and this is a rational premise - for if the body moved because it is a body, then every body would be moving. And every mover is either emanating from the essence of the moved, like nature in the motion of stone downward, and will in the motion of the animal by power, or the mover is external, but it moves by way of compulsion, like pushing a stone upward. And everything that moves in meaning in itself either does not perceive that thing - and we call it nature, like the motion of a stone downward - or perceives it - and we call it volitional and psychic. So motion, by these present divisions, oscillating between denial and affirmation, becomes either compulsory, or natural, or volitional. And if two divisions are invalid, the third is determined. And it cannot be compulsory, because the compelling mover is either a body or another body that moves by will or by compulsion, and it necessarily ends in will, and if it is established that among the bodies of the heavens there is a mover by will, then the purpose is achieved; so what benefit is there in positing compulsory motions, and ultimately one must return to will? Or it could be said that it moves by compulsion, and God Most High is the mover without intermediary, and that is impossible, because if it moved by Him insofar as it is a body and He is its Creator, then every body would necessarily move; so motion must be specified by an attribute by which it is distinguished from other bodies, and that attribute is the proximate mover, which is either will or nature. And it cannot be said that God Most High moves it by will, because His will relates to bodies in one relation, so why was this body specifically chosen for its motion to be willed without others? And that cannot be arbitrary, for that is impossible, as previously in the issue of the world's origination. And if it is established that this body must have an attribute that is the principle of motion, the first division, which is the supposition of compulsory motion, becomes invalid. So it remains to be said: it is natural, and that is not possible, because nature by itself is absolutely not a cause for motion, for the meaning of motion is escape from a place and seeking another place. So the place in which the body is, if it is suitable for it, it does not move from it; and for this reason a wineskin filled with air does not move downward upon the water's surface, and if it is immersed in water, it moves to the water's surface because it finds the suitable place, so it rests, and nature is with it.
Source pages p. 218

Commentary
This passage is a combined presentation and critique of the philosophers' claim that the heavens are animate beings that worship God through their rotational motion. Ghazali begins by stating the issue: the philosophers assert that the heavens have souls analogous to ours and move by will toward worship. He acknowledges that this is not impossible, since God can create life in any body regardless of size or shape. However, he immediately declares that they cannot prove this by rational proof (دليل العقل); such knowledge, if true, comes only through prophecy or inspiration. He then presents their attempted rational proof, which he calls their "fallacy" (خبالهم). The proof proceeds as follows: (1) The heavens are in motion (sensory premise). (2) Every moving body has a mover (rational premise, otherwise all bodies would move). (3) The mover is either internal (nature or will) or external (compulsion). (4) Internal movers are either without perception (nature) or with perception (will/psychic). (5) Motion is thus either compulsory, natural, or volitional. Then they argue that compulsory motion is impossible: either it leads to an infinite regress of movers, or it ends in will (so the purpose is achieved), or if God moves it directly without intermediary, then because His will relates equally to all bodies, no body would be specified for motion without an attribute—so there must be a proximate mover (will or nature). Thus compulsory motion fails. Natural motion is also impossible because nature only moves a body toward its natural place; once there, it rests. The heavens are always moving, so their motion cannot be natural. Therefore, by elimination, the motion must be volitional, which supports the philosophers' claim that the heavens have souls and move by will. However, Ghazali's point is that this reasoning only yields opinion (ظن), not certainty (قطع). The argument continues beyond the window.

Key Terms
تحريك (motion)
قسري (compulsory)
طبيعي (natural)
إرادي (volitional)
نفس (soul)
حيوان (living being)
دليل العقل (rational proof)
ظن (opinion)
قطع (certainty)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The philosophers' proof provides certain knowledge that the heavens are alive.
Correction: Ghazali argues the proof only yields opinion (ظن), not certainty (قطع). Such metaphysical truths are known through prophecy, not rational analogy alone.

The philosophers' argument that the heavens are animate relies on an elimination of motion types, but Ghazali shows it yields only probable opinion, not certain knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about the heavens moving in perfect order, does your heart feel certainty about God's wisdom, or do you rely on rational arguments that might only be probable?
?
Have you ever been so convinced by a logical argument about your faith that you forgot it is merely a human construct, not the infallible revelation from God?
?
If the philosophers' elaborate proof falls short of certainty, what does that mean for your own attempts to prove God's attributes through reason alone? Will you humbly accept the limits of your intellect and seek certainty from revelation instead?

Classification of Motion into Three Types
Ghazali categorizes motion into three types with definitions and examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's threefold classification of motion: forced, natural, and voluntary.

Core Idea
Motion is either coerced, natural, or voluntary — three distinct categories.

Ghazali begins by establishing a clear classification of motion, which is essential for his argument about celestial motion. He defines each type by its relationship to the mover and the perception of the moving object.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types
Read the passage and list the three types of motion Ghazali names: forced, natural, and voluntary. Notice the criteria he uses to distinguish them.
Pay attention to the role of perception in natural vs. voluntary motion.
2
Understand the definitions
For each type, note the definition: forced motion has an external mover acting by coercion; natural motion occurs without perception, driven by inherent nature; voluntary motion involves perception and will.
Examples: stone falling (natural), stone pushed up (forced), animal movement (voluntary).
3
See the overall division
Ghazali concludes that all motion falls under these three divisions. This sets the stage for proving that celestial motion must be one of them.
Remember that celestial motion cannot be forced or natural, as shown in the next lesson.

حركة الحجر إلى أسفل ، والإرادة في حركة الحيوان القدرة ، وإما أن يكون مع المحرك خارجاً ، ولكن يحرك عن طريق القسر ، كدفع الحجر إلى فوق بالحركة ، وكل ما يتحرك بمعنى في ذاته ، فإما أن لا يشعر ذلك الشيء للزم أن يتحرك كل جسم ، فلابد أن تختص الحركة بصفة ، بها يتميز عن غيره من الأجسام وتلك الصفة هي المحرك القريب ، فإما الإرادة أو الطبع ، ولا يمكن أن يقال : إن الله تعالى يحركه بالإرادة ، لأن إرادته تناسب الأجسام نسبة واحدة ، فلم استعد هذا الجسم على الخصوص، لأن يراد تحريكه دون غيره ، ولا يمكن أن يكون ذلك جزافاً ، فإن ذلك محال ، كما سبق في مسألة حدوث العالم . وإذا ثبت أن هذا الجسم ، ينبغي أن يكون فيه صفة تكون فيه صفة هي مبدأ الحركة . بطل القسم الأول ، وهو تقدير الحركة القسرية فيبقى أن يقال : هي طبيعية ، وهو غير ممكن ، لأن الطبيعة بمجردها ، قطعاً لا تكون سبباً للحركة ، لأن معنى الحركة ، هرب من مكان ، وطلب لمكان آخر ، فالمكان الذي فيه الجسم ، إن كان ملائماً له ، فلا يتحرك عنه ؛ ولهذا لا يتحرك زق مملوء من الهواء ، على وجه الماء ، إلى أسفل ، وإذا غمس في الماء ، تحرك إلى وجه الماء ، لأنه وجد المكان الملائم ، فسكن ، والطبيعة معه قائمة ، ولكن إن نقل إلى مكان لا يلائمه ، هرب منه إلى الملائم ، كما هرب المملوء بالهواء ، من وسط الماء ، إلى حيز الهواء والحركة الدورية ، لا أن تكون طبيعية ، لأن كل وضع وأين يتصور يفرض الهرب منه ، فهو عائد إليه ، والمهروب منه بالطبع ، لا يكون مطلوباً ، ولذلك لا ينصرف زق مملوء من الهواء ، إلى باطن الماء ، ولا الحجر بالطبع ينصرف ، بعد الاستقرار على الأرض ، فيعود إلى الهواء . فلم يبق إلا القسم الثالث ، وهو الحركة الإرادية
The motion of a stone downward, and the will in the motion of an animal [is] power. And sometimes the mover is external, but moves by way of coercion, like pushing a stone upward by motion. And everything that moves in a sense in itself: either it does not perceive that thing, and we call it nature, like the motion of a stone downward; or it perceives it, and we call it voluntary and psychic. So motion by these present divisions, circulating between negation and affirmation, becomes either coerced, or natural, or voluntary. And if two divisions are invalidated, the third is determined. And it cannot be coerced, because the coercing mover, either a body or another body, moves by will or by coercion, and necessarily ends at will. And whenever it is established that in the celestial bodies there is a mover by will, the goal is attained. So what benefit is there in positing coerced motions, ultimately having to return to will? And if it is said that it moves by coercion, and God most high is the mover without intermediary, that is impossible, because if it moved by Him insofar as it is a body and He is its creator, it would necessitate that every body moves. So the motion must be specified by a quality by which it is distinguished from other bodies, and that quality is the proximate mover, either will or nature. And it cannot be said that God most high moves it by will, because His will applies to bodies with a single proportion, so why was this particular body singled out to be moved rather than another? And it cannot be that [it is] arbitrary, for that is impossible, as preceded in the issue of the world's origination. And if it is established that this body should have a quality that is the principle of motion, the first division is invalidated, i.e., supposing coerced motion. Then it remains to be said: it is natural, and that is not possible, because nature by itself is absolutely not a cause of motion, for the meaning of motion is fleeing from a place and seeking another place. So the place in which the body is, if it is suitable for it, it does not move from it; and for this reason a waterskin filled with air does not move downward on the surface of water, and if it is submerged in water, it moves to the surface of the water because it found the suitable place, and it rested, while nature remains with it. But if it is transferred to a place not suitable for it, it flees from it to the suitable, as the air-filled [waterskin] fled from the middle of water to the air space. And circular motion cannot be natural, because every position and place from which it is imagined to flee, it returns to it, and that which is naturally fled from is not sought. Therefore an air-filled waterskin does not go to the interior of water, nor does a stone naturally return to the air after settling on the ground. So nothing remains except the third division, which is voluntary motion.
Source pages p. 219

Commentary
This passage is a classification and proof. Ghazali begins by distinguishing three types of motion: forced (qasriyya), natural (tabi'iyya), and voluntary (irādiyya). He then argues that the motion of the celestial spheres cannot be forced, because any forced motion ultimately traces back to a voluntary mover, leading to an infinite regress unless we accept a voluntary mover in the heavens. If one says God directly forces the heavens without a proximate cause, Ghazali refutes it: God's will applies equally to all bodies, so no specification of motion to one body could occur without a qualifying attribute in that body, which would be either will or nature. Since forced motion is impossible, he examines natural motion. A natural mover moves only toward a suitable place; circular motion, however, continuously returns to positions from which it flees, which contradicts the definition of natural motion (which seeks only one suitable place and rests). Therefore, the only option left is voluntary motion, implying that the heavens are animate and move by their own will.

قسرية
طبيعية
إرادية

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think carefully: Which of your actions today were truly voluntary—chosen with full awareness and intention—and which were merely natural habits or forced by circumstance? What would it take to make more of your motions voluntary acts of obedience to Allah?
?
If celestial motion is voluntary and reflects the will of its Creator, what does the regularity of your daily life—your routines, reactions, and choices—reveal about the state of your inner will? Are you moving with conscious purpose or on autopilot?
?
The stone falls without choice; you are not a stone. But how often do you act like one, following the path of least resistance without reflecting on whether your motion is coerced by desires, natural by habit, or truly voluntary in submission to Allah? What is one area where you will reclaim your will today?

Causality and Created Things
intermediate

Proving Celestial Motion is Voluntary
Ghazali refutes forced and natural motion for celestial bodies, proving voluntary motion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's argument that celestial motion must be voluntary because it cannot be forced or natural.

Core Idea
Celestial motion must be voluntary because forced motion regresses and natural motion seeks rest.

Having classified motion, Ghazali now applies this to celestial spheres. He systematically eliminates forced and natural motion, concluding that the heavens must move by their own will.

Guided Path
1
Refute forced motion
Read the argument against forced motion: any forced motion requires an external mover, which itself must move by will or force, leading to an infinite regress. If one says God directly forces, then all bodies would move equally, so a specifying quality (will or nature) must be in the body.
Forced motion is impossible for celestial bodies.
2
Refute natural motion
Read the argument against natural motion: natural motion seeks a suitable place and rests there. Circular motion (like celestial spheres) continuously returns to positions it flees from, which contradicts the definition of natural motion.
Circular motion cannot be natural.
3
Conclude voluntary motion
With forced and natural motion eliminated, only voluntary motion remains. This implies celestial bodies are animate and move by their own will.
This supports Ghazali's broader critique of Aristotelian physics.

الغرض ؛ فأي فائدة في وضع حركات قسرية ، وبالآخرة لابد من الرجوع إلى الإرادة ؟ وإما أن يقال إنه يتحرك بالقسر ، والله تعالى هو المحرك بغير واسطة ، وهو محال ، لأنه لو تحرك به من حيث إنه جسم ، وإنه خالقه ، للزم أن يتحرك كل جسم ، فلابد أن تختص الحركة بصفة ، بها يتميز عن غيره من الأجسام وتلك الصفة هي المحرك القريب ، فإما الإرادة أو الطبع ، ولا يمكن أن يقال : إن الله تعالى يحركه بالإرادة ، لأن إرادته تناسب الأجسام نسبة واحدة ، فلم استعد هذا الجسم على الخصوص، لأن يراد تحريكه دون غيره ، ولا يمكن أن يكون ذلك جزافاً ، فإن ذلك محال ، كما سبق في مسألة حدوث العالم . وإذا ثبت أن هذا الجسم ، ينبغي أن يكون فيه صفة تكون فيه صفة هي مبدأ الحركة . بطل القسم الأول ، وهو تقدير الحركة القسرية فيبقى أن يقال : هي طبيعية ، وهو غير ممكن ، لأن الطبيعة بمجردها ، قطعاً لا تكون سبباً للحركة ، لأن معنى الحركة ، هرب من مكان ، وطلب لمكان آخر ، فالمكان الذي فيه الجسم ، إن كان ملائماً له ، فلا يتحرك عنه ؛ ولهذا لا يتحرك زق مملوء من الهواء ، على وجه الماء ، إلى أسفل ، وإذا غمس في الماء ، تحرك إلى وجه الماء ، لأنه وجد المكان الملائم ، فسكن ، والطبيعة معه قائمة ، ولكن إن نقل إلى مكان لا يلائمه ، هرب منه إلى الملائم ، كما هرب المملوء بالهواء ، من وسط الماء ، إلى حيز الهواء والحركة الدورية ، لا أن تكون طبيعية ، لأن كل وضع وأين يتصور يفرض الهرب منه ، فهو عائد إليه ، والمهروب منه بالطبع ، لا يكون مطلوباً ، ولذلك لا ينصرف زق مملوء من الهواء ، إلى باطن الماء ، ولا الحجر بالطبع ينصرف ، بعد الاستقرار على الأرض ، فيعود إلى الهواء . فلم يبق إلا القسم الثالث ، وهو الحركة الإرادية
The motion of a stone downward, and the will in the motion of an animal [is] power. And sometimes the mover is external, but moves by way of coercion, like pushing a stone upward by motion. And everything that moves in a sense in itself: either it does not perceive that thing, and we call it nature, like the motion of a stone downward; or it perceives it, and we call it voluntary and psychic. So motion by these present divisions, circulating between negation and affirmation, becomes either coerced, or natural, or voluntary. And if two divisions are invalidated, the third is determined. And it cannot be coerced, because the coercing mover, either a body or another body, moves by will or by coercion, and necessarily ends at will. And whenever it is established that in the celestial bodies there is a mover by will, the goal is attained. So what benefit is there in positing coerced motions, ultimately having to return to will? And if it is said that it moves by coercion, and God most high is the mover without intermediary, that is impossible, because if it moved by Him insofar as it is a body and He is its creator, it would necessitate that every body moves. So the motion must be specified by a quality by which it is distinguished from other bodies, and that quality is the proximate mover, either will or nature. And it cannot be said that God most high moves it by will, because His will applies to bodies with a single proportion, so why was this particular body singled out to be moved rather than another? And it cannot be that [it is] arbitrary, for that is impossible, as preceded in the issue of the world's origination. And if it is established that this body should have a quality that is the principle of motion, the first division is invalidated, i.e., supposing coerced motion. Then it remains to be said: it is natural, and that is not possible, because nature by itself is absolutely not a cause of motion, for the meaning of motion is fleeing from a place and seeking another place. So the place in which the body is, if it is suitable for it, it does not move from it; and for this reason a waterskin filled with air does not move downward on the surface of water, and if it is submerged in water, it moves to the surface of the water because it found the suitable place, and it rested, while nature remains with it. But if it is transferred to a place not suitable for it, it flees from it to the suitable, as the air-filled [waterskin] fled from the middle of water to the air space. And circular motion cannot be natural, because every position and place from which it is imagined to flee, it returns to it, and that which is naturally fled from is not sought. Therefore an air-filled waterskin does not go to the interior of water, nor does a stone naturally return to the air after settling on the ground. So nothing remains except the third division, which is voluntary motion.
Source pages p. 219

Commentary
This passage is a classification and proof. Ghazali begins by distinguishing three types of motion: forced (qasriyya), natural (tabi'iyya), and voluntary (irādiyya). He then argues that the motion of the celestial spheres cannot be forced, because any forced motion ultimately traces back to a voluntary mover, leading to an infinite regress unless we accept a voluntary mover in the heavens. If one says God directly forces the heavens without a proximate cause, Ghazali refutes it: God's will applies equally to all bodies, so no specification of motion to one body could occur without a qualifying attribute in that body, which would be either will or nature. Since forced motion is impossible, he examines natural motion. A natural mover moves only toward a suitable place; circular motion, however, continuously returns to positions from which it flees, which contradicts the definition of natural motion (which seeks only one suitable place and rests). Therefore, the only option left is voluntary motion, implying that the heavens are animate and move by their own will.

Key Terms
قسرية
طبيعية
إرادية
محرك
جسم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think celestial motion is natural because it appears regular and constant.
Correction: Ghazali shows that natural motion always seeks a suitable place and rests, whereas circular motion never rests and returns to positions it flees, contradicting the definition of natural motion.

Checkpoint
What is one reason Ghazali gives against forced celestial motion?
A) It requires an infinite chain of movers
B) It would make all bodies move identically if God directly forces
C) It is impossible for God to cause motion directly
D) Both A and B

Key Takeaway
By eliminating forced and natural motion, Ghazali establishes that celestial bodies move voluntarily, implying they are animate.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have learned that celestial motion is voluntary, implying that the heavens are animate and choose their movement. Do you live as if the universe worships God consciously, or do you treat creation as dead matter? What would change in your daily attitude if you truly believed every star and planet moves by will and praise?
?
Ghazali shows that natural motion seeks rest, but celestial motion never rests. Are you seeking rest in this life, or are you moving perpetually towards the Hereafter? In what area of your life have you settled for a 'rest' that might actually be a spiritual stagnation?
?
The argument eliminated forced motion because it would make God's will arbitrary. Have you ever acted as if God's commands are arbitrary or without wisdom? How can this lesson about the precise specification in creation increase your trust in the wisdom of every divine decree?

Objection to Celestial Voluntary Motion and Ghazali's Response
The objector presents two alternatives to Ghazali's view, and Ghazali gives a partial response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two alternatives proposed by the objector against Ghazali's claim that celestial motion is voluntary, and Ghazali's initial rebuttal that the objection's logic turns back on itself.

Core Idea
Objections against divine will often assume what they aim to disprove.

In this passage, an objector raises two counter-proposals to Ghazali's argument that the heaven moves by its own will. The first suggests an external mover; the second attributes all motion to God's direct creation. Ghazali responds briefly, pointing out that the very reasoning used by the objector actually supports his own position. However, the text cuts off before his full response, leaving the third alternative unstated.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice the structure: the objector presents two alternatives, and Ghazali responds.
The passage ends abruptly; note the missing third alternative.
2
Identify the two alternatives
Find the first alternative (another body moving the heaven) and the second alternative (all motion forced by God). Why does the objector think these alternatives undermine Ghazali's claim?
The second alternative includes the example of a stone falling.
3
Understand Ghazali's response
Ghazali argues that the specification problem (why will attaches to one thing over another) applies to the objector's own view. He says the objector's 'improbability' turns back on them.
The response is incomplete; it cuts off after 'for we say: and why'.
4
Reflect on the argumentative strategy
Consider how Ghazali turns the objection around. Instead of defending his position directly, he shows that the objector's alternative leads to the same difficulty.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاعتراض ، هو أن نقول : نحن نقدر ثلاثة احتمالات ، سوى مذهبكم ، لا برهان على بطلانها الأول : أن تقدر حركة السماء قهراً ، بجسم آخر ، مريد لحركتها ، يديرها على الدوام . وذلك الجسم المحرك ، لا يكون كرة ، ولا يكون محيطاً، فلا يكون سماء فيبطل قولهم : إن حركة السماء إرادية ، وإن السماء حيوان ، وهذا الذي ذكرناه ، ممكن الوجود ، وليس في دفعه إلا مجرد الاستبعاد . أن يقال : الحركة قسرية ، ومبدؤها إرادة الله تعالى ، فإنا الثاني : هو نقول : حركة الحجر إلى أسفل أيضاً قسرية تحدث بخلق الله تعالى فيه الحركة ، وكذا القول في سائر حركات الأجسام ، التي ليست حيوانية فيبقى استبعادهم ، أن الإرادة لم اختصت به ؟ وسائر الأجسام تشاركها في الجسمية . فقد بينا ، أن الإرادة القديمة، من شأنها تخصيص الشيء عن مثله ، وأنهم مضطرون إلى إثبات صفة هذا شأنها ، في تعيين جهة الحركة الدورية ، وفي تعيين موضع القطب والنقطة ، فلا نعيده والقول الوجيز ، أن ما استبعدوه في اختصاص الجسم ، بتعلق الإرادة به من غير تميز بصفة ، ينقلب عليهم ، في تميزه بتلك الصفة ، فإنا نقول : ولم
The objection is that we say: We assume three possibilities besides your doctrine, with no proof of their falsity. First: that one suppose the motion of the heaven is forced by another body, willing its motion, rotating it perpetually. And that moving body is not a sphere, nor encompassing, thus it is not a heaven, so their statement is invalidated that the motion of the heaven is voluntary and that the heaven is an animal. And what we have mentioned is possible of existence, and there is nothing in refuting it except mere improbability. Second: we say: The motion of a stone downward is also forced, occurring by God's creation of motion in it, and likewise the statement concerning all motions of bodies that are not animal. So their improbability remains: why is the will particular to it? And all bodies share in corporeality. We have already shown that the eternal will has the property of specifying a thing from its like, and that they are compelled to affirm a quality with this property in determining the direction of circular motion and in determining the position of the pole and the point, so we will not repeat it. And the succinct statement is that what they deem improbable in the specification of the body by the attachment of will to it without distinction by a quality, turns back on them in its distinction by that quality, for we say: and why...
Source pages p. 219

Commentary
This passage presents an objection (i`tirāḍ) against Ghazali's proof that celestial motion is voluntary. The objector proposes three alternatives. The first is that the heaven could be forced by another body (a non-heavenly mover) that rotates it perpetually; this possibility would invalidate the claim that the heaven is an animal moving by its own will. The objector says this is possible and only rejected by mere improbability. The second alternative is that all motion (including a stone falling) is forced by God's direct creation, so the heaven's motion could similarly be forced by God. The objector points out that the issue of why God's will specifies one body over another remains. Ghazali responds briefly: he has already proven that eternal will necessarily specifies without a qualifying attribute in the object (as in the case of the direction of motion and the position of the pole), and that the same logic applies here. His succinct rebuttal is that the very specification they object to (will attaching to a body without a prior quality) is actually what they themselves must accept when they posit a quality distinguishing that body, for that quality would itself need specification by will. The text cuts off mid-sentence, but the argument is that the alternative leads to the same problem.

Key Takeaway
The objector's attempt to avoid voluntary celestial motion fails because any alternative either implies another mind (first alternative) or encounters the same specification problem (second alternative).

First Possibility: Motion by Coercion from Another Body
Ghazali presents the first of three possible alternatives and dismisses its refutation by improbability.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first alternative to the philosophers' claim about celestial motion and why improbability is not a rational refutation.

Core Idea
Improbability is not a proof; even a far-fetched alternative can be logically possible.

Imam al-Ghazali begins his critique of the philosophers' assertion that the heavens are animate by presenting possible alternatives that are logically conceivable. Here, he introduces the first alternative: the heaven's motion might be forced by another body. This possibility, though remote, cannot be refuted by mere improbability. In this lesson, we will explore this alternative and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Understand the context of the objection
Read the passage to see how Ghazali frames his objection. He claims there are three possibilities besides the philosophers' view, and he will refute each.
Notice the phrase 'لا برهان على بطلانها' – no proof for their invalidity.
2
Examine the first possibility: coerced motion
Focus on the description of the first alternative: motion by coercion from another body that wills the motion. What does this imply about the nature of the heavens?
The moving body is not a sphere and not surrounding, so it is not a heaven.
3
Reflect on the role of improbability
Ghazali notes that the only response to this possibility is 'mere improbability' (مجرد الاستبعاد). Consider why improbability is not a valid logical refutation.
This insight is central to Ghazali's critique of philosophical certainty.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاعتراض ، هو أن نقول : نحن نقدر ثلاثة احتمالات ، سوى مذهبكم ، لا برهان على بطلانها . الأول : أن تقدر حركة السماء قهراً ، بجسم آخر ، مريد لحركتها ، يديرها على الدوام . وذلك الجسم المحرك ، لا يكون كرة ، ولا يكون محيطاً، فلا يكون سماء فيبطل قولهم : إن حركة السماء إرادية ، وإن السماء حيوان ، وهذا الذى ذكرناه ، ممكن الوجود ، وليس فى دفعه إلا مجرد الاستبعاد .
The objection is that we say: we can conceive three possibilities besides your doctrine, with no proof for their invalidity. The first: that you suppose the motion of the heaven is by coercion, by another body that wills its motion and turns it perpetually. And that moving body is not a sphere and not surrounding, so it is not a heaven, and thus their statement that the motion of the heaven is voluntary and that the heaven is an animal is nullified. And this which we have mentioned is possible in existence, and there is nothing in refuting it except mere improbability.
Source pages p. 220

Commentary
This passage introduces a formal objection against the philosophers' claim that the heavens are animate beings with voluntary motion. The objector proposes three alternative explanations that are logically possible and for which no demonstrative proof exists to refute them. The first alternative suggests that the heaven's motion might be coerced by another body—a mover that wills the motion and perpetually rotates the heavens. This moving body is not spherical and does not surround the universe, so it is not a heaven itself. This nullifies the philosophers' conclusion that the heaven's motion is voluntary and that the heaven is an animal. However, Ghazali notes that this alternative is ontologically possible; the only thing against it is mere improbability (istib'ad), not a rational proof. The passage thus shows that the philosophers' claim is not necessary but merely one possibility among others.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Improbability is a sufficient rational refutation.
Correction: Ghazali argues that improbability is not a proof; something can be improbable yet still possible. The philosophers must provide a demonstrative proof to rule out alternatives.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The first alternative to the philosophers' view is logically possible and cannot be refuted by mere improbability; a real proof is required.

Third Possibility: Motion by an Inherent Quality Without Consciousness
Ghazali presents the third alternative and refutes the philosophers' objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's third alternative for celestial motion and how he refutes the philosophers' objection that circular motion requires consciousness or place-seeking.

Core Idea
Circular motion can be caused by an inherent quality without any perception or consciousness.

Ghazali presents a third possibility: the heaven's motion could arise from an inherent quality similar to a stone's fall, without consciousness. He then refutes the philosophers' objection that all motion is for seeking or fleeing a place, showing that circular motion could be directly necessitated by a created meaning. This lesson examines that argument and its implications for the nature of celestial motion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Carefully read the Arabic passage once, then the literal English translation. Notice the structure: third possibility presented, then an objection, then Ghazali's rebuttal, and a conclusion.
The passage is dense; take your time to parse the logical flow.
2
Identify the third alternative
Find where Ghazali concedes that the heaven has a quality that is the principle of motion, like a stone's tendency to fall, but without consciousness. Understand that this is an alternative to the philosophers' claim of voluntary motion.
Ghazali uses the example of a stone's natural motion to illustrate an unconscious inherent quality.
3
Analyze the refutation
Focus on Ghazali's response to the philosophers' objection that what is naturally sought is not naturally fled from. He argues that since the universe is one body and circular motion is continuous, there is no actual distinction of places. Therefore motion need not be for seeking or fleeing; it can be directly necessitated by a created meaning.
Note how Ghazali challenges the necessity of the philosophers' premise about all motion being place-seeking or fleeing.
4
Conclude the argument
Ghazali ends by stating that the philosophers' claim that the heaven is an animal with voluntary motion is not certain; it is merely a probable opinion without proof. This highlights the limits of rational inference.
The key takeaway: the philosophers have not proven their case; other possibilities remain.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث ، هو : أنا نسلم أن السماء ، اختصت بصفة ، تلك الصفة مبدأ الحركة ، كما اعتقدوه فى هوى الحجر إلى أسفل ، إلا أنها لا تشعر بها ، كالحجر . وقولهم : إن المطلوب بالطبع ، لا يكون مهر وباً منه بالطبع ، فتلبيس ، لأنه ليس ثم أماكن متفاضلة بالعدد عندهم ، بل الجسم واحد ، والحركة الدورية واحدة ، فلا للجسم جزء بالفعل ، ولا للحركة جزء بالفعل ، وإنما تتجزأ بالوهم ، فليست تلك الحركة ، لطلب مكان ، ولا للهرب من مكان ، فيمكن أن يخلق في ذاته معنى يقتضى حركة دورية ، وتكون الحركة نفسها ، مقتضى ذلك المعنى ، لا أن مقتضى المعنى طلب المكان ، ثم تكون حركة للوصول إليه . وقولكم : إن كل حركة ، فهى لطلب مكان ، أو هرب منه ، إذا كان ضرورياً ، فكأنكم جعلتم طلب المكان ، مقتضى الطبع . وجعلتم الحركة غير مقصودة في نفسها ، بل وسيلة إليه ، ونحن نقول : لا يبعد ، أن تكون الحركة نفس المقتضى ، لا طلب المكان ، فما الذي يحيل ذلك ؟ قطعاً . فاستبان أن ما ذكروه ، إن ظن أنه أغلب من احتمال آخر ، فلا يتيقن ، انتفاء غيره ، فالحكم على السماء بأنها حيوان ، تحكم محض ، لا مستند له .
The third is: we concede that the heaven is distinguished by a quality, that quality being the principle of motion, as they believed in the falling of the stone downward, except that it does not perceive it, like the stone. And their statement: 'What is naturally sought is not naturally fled from' is obfuscation, because according to them there are no places differing in number; rather the body is one and the circular motion is one. So neither does the body have a part in actuality nor does the motion have a part in actuality; it is only divided by estimation. So that motion is not for seeking a place nor for fleeing from a place. So it is possible that in its essence be created a meaning (ma'na) that necessitates circular motion, and the motion itself is the necessitation of that meaning, not that the necessitation of the meaning is the seeking of a place, then motion occurs to reach it. And your statement: 'Every motion is either for seeking a place or fleeing from it', if it were necessary, it would be as if you made place-seeking a necessitation of nature, and you made motion not intended in itself but a means to it. And we say: it is not unlikely that motion itself is the necessitated thing, not place-seeking. So what prevents that? Absolutely. Thus it became clear that what they mentioned, if they think it more probable than another possibility, they are not certain of the non-existence of others. So the judgment that the heaven is an animal is pure arbitrary judgment with no basis.
Source pages p. 220

Commentary
This passage presents the third alternative and concludes the refutation. The third possibility admits that the heaven has a specific quality that is the source of its circular motion, similar to the stone's tendency to fall downward, but without the heaven having consciousness of that quality. The philosophers object that what is naturally sought (like a place) is not naturally fled from, implying that motion cannot be circular since it would involve both seeking and fleeing. Ghazali argues that this is obfuscation because, in their own system, places are not differentiated in number; the universe is one body and circular motion is one continuous motion with no actual parts. Thus the motion is not for seeking or fleeing a particular place. It can be that a created meaning (ma'na) directly necessitates circular motion itself, not as a means to reach a place. The philosophers' claim that all motion is for seeking or fleeing a place is not necessary; motion could be directly intended. Therefore, their conclusion that the heaven is an animal with voluntary motion is not certain—it is merely a subjective preference without proof.

مبدأ الحركة
شعور
معنى
حركة دورية
مهروب

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that circular motion implies consciousness or a voluntary mover, as the philosophers claimed.
Correction: Ghazali argues that circular motion can be caused by an inherent quality without consciousness, just as a stone falls naturally without perception.

Checkpoint
What is Ghazali's main point in refuting the philosophers' claim that circular motion must be voluntary?
A) Circular motion requires a sentient mover.
B) Motion can be directly caused by an inherent quality without place-seeking.
C) The heaven is actually stationary.
D) The philosophers have a valid proof.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali argues that the heaven's circular motion could be directly caused by an inherent quality, not requiring consciousness or place-seeking, so the philosophers' proof for the heaven's life is inconclusive.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just read Ghazali's argument that the heavens' motion could be caused by an inherent quality without consciousness. Have you ever assumed that everything with movement must have a conscious purpose? Where in your life have you fallen into this same trap, attributing intention where there may be none, or missing the direct action of Allah because you looked for a created cause? Will you now pause and ask Allah to show you where He is directly acting without the intermediaries you assume?
?
Ghazali refutes the philosophers' claim that all motion is for seeking or fleeing. In your own daily actions, do you often assume that every movement or change has a conscious goal? How might you be overlooking the simple, direct decrees of Allah in your life's apparent purposes? What would it mean for you to submit to Allah's direct causation even when you cannot see a personal purpose?
?
The philosophers' judgment about the heaven being an animal is called 'pure arbitrary judgment with no basis' by Ghazali. Think of a belief you hold about Allah's creation that you have never critically examined. Could it be based on assumption rather than evidence? Are you willing to challenge that assumption today, even if it unsettles you?

Infinite Regress in Celestial Distinction Necessitates Will
Ghazali argues that positing a distinguishing quality leads to infinite regress, proving the need for will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali uses the infinite regress argument to prove that the celestial sphere's unique motion requires a will, not merely natural necessity.

Core Idea
The sky's unique motion cannot be explained without a willing agent.

This lesson examines Ghazali's argument against the philosophers who try to explain the distinct motion of the celestial sphere through natural attributes. Ghazali shows that any such explanation leads to an infinite regress, ultimately forcing the admission of a will. Additionally, he critiques the notion that all motion must be for seeking or fleeing a place, opening the door for a direct, non-purposive motion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the use of 'ثم' and 'فإن' that signal logical steps.
2
Trace the infinite regress argument
Identify the starting point: the sky's distinguishing attribute. Follow the logic: if explained by another attribute, the question repeats. What is the conclusion?
This is the core of the first part.
3
Analyze the third point about motion
Even if we concede a natural attribute for motion, Ghazali argues that celestial motion need not be for seeking or fleeing a place. How does he support this?
Contrast with the stone's fall.
4
Reflect on the outcome
Ghazali concludes that the philosophers' claim that the sky is an animal is baseless. Why is their certainty undermined?
Think about the role of probability vs. certainty.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تميز جسم السماء بتلك الصفة ، التي بها فارق غيره من الأجسام ، وسائر الأجسام أيضاً أجسام ، فلم حصل فيه ، ما لم يحصل في غيره ؟ فإن علل ذلك بصفة أخرى ، توجه السؤال فى الصفة الأخرى ، وهكذا يتسلسل إلى غير نهاية فيضطرون بالآخرة ، إلى الحكم بالإرادة ، وأن في المبادئ ما يميز الشيء عن مثله ، ويخصصه بصفة عن أمثالها . الثالث [؟] ، هو أنا نسلم أن السماء اختصت بصفة ، تلك الصفة مبدأ الحركة ، كما اعتقدوه فى هوى الحجر إلى أسفل ، إلا أنها لا تشعر بها ، كالحجر . وقولهم : إن المطلوب بالطبع ، لا يكون مهر وباً منه بالطبع [؟] ، فتلبيس ، لأنه ليس ثم أماكن متفاضلة بالعدد عندهم ، بل الجسم واحد ، والحركة الدورية واحدة ، فلا للجسم جزء بالفعل ، ولا للحركة جزء بالفعل ، وإنما تتجزأ بالوهم ، فليست تلك الحركة لطلب مكان ، ولا للهرب من مكان ، فيمكن أن يخلق في ذاته معنى يقتضى حركة دورية ، وتكون الحركة نفسها مقتضى ذلك المعنى ، لا أن مقتضى المعنى طلب المكان ، ثم تكون حركة للوصول إليه . وقولكم : إن كل حركة فهى لطلب مكان أو هرب منه ، إذا كان ضرورياً ، فكأنكم جعلتم طلب المكان مقتضى الطبع . وجعلتم الحركة غير مقصودة في نفسها ، بل وسيلة إليه ، ونحن نقول : لا يبعد أن تكون الحركة نفس المقتضى ، لا طلب المكان ، فما الذي يحيل ذلك ؟ فاستبان أن ما ذكروه إن ظن أنه أغلب من احتمال آخر ، فلا يتيقن انتفاء غيره ، فالحكم على السماء بأنها حيوان تحكم محض لا مستند له .
The body of the sky is distinguished by that attribute by which it differs from other bodies, while all other bodies are also bodies. So why did it acquire what others did not? If that is explained by another attribute, the question applies to that other attribute, and so on in infinite regress. Thus they are compelled in the end to judge by will, and that there is in the principles something that distinguishes a thing from its like and specifies it with an attribute over its counterparts. The third [؟]: We concede that the sky is distinguished by an attribute which is the principle of motion, as they believe in the falling of a stone downwards, except that it does not perceive it, like the stone. And their saying: 'That which is sought by nature is not an escape from it by nature' is obfuscation, because according to them there are no places differing in number; rather the body is one, and the circular motion is one. So the body has no actual part, nor does the motion have an actual part; they are only divided by imagination. So that motion is not for seeking a place, nor for fleeing a place. So it is possible that a meaning is created in its essence that necessitates circular motion, and the motion itself is the requirement of that meaning, not that the requirement of the meaning is seeking a place, then motion occurs to reach it. And your saying: 'Every motion is for seeking a place or fleeing from it, if it is necessary', then you made seeking a place the requirement of nature, and you made motion unintended in itself but a means to it. We say: It is not unlikely that motion itself is the requirement, not seeking a place; so what prevents that? So it is clear that what they mentioned, if they think it is more probable than another possibility, they are not certain of the absence of other alternatives. So the judgment that the sky is an animal is a pure judgment with no basis.
Source pages p. 221

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the philosophers' attempt to explain the distinctive motion of the celestial sphere without invoking will. Ghazali begins by posing the problem: the sky is distinguished by an attribute that makes it different from other bodies, but since all bodies are bodies, why does this attribute belong to the sky alone? If they try to explain it by another attribute, the same question applies to that attribute, leading to an infinite regress. Therefore, they are forced to accept that there is a will that specifies one thing over another. Then he presents a third point: even if we concede that the sky has an attribute that is the principle of motion (like the stone's natural downward motion), the motion is not perceived by the sky. The philosophers say that what is sought by nature is not an escape from it, but Ghazali calls this obfuscation because according to them, there are no distinct places; the body and its circular motion are one undivided entity. So the motion is not for seeking or fleeing a place. Instead, a meaning could be created in the sky that directly necessitates circular motion, without the motion being a means to an end. He counters the claim that all motion is for seeking or fleeing a place, arguing that motion can be the direct requirement of that meaning. Since this is possible, their certainty that the sky is an animal (with will) is baseless.

إرادة
طبع
حركة
مكان
تسلسل
مقتضى
