Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All motion must be for seeking or fleeing a place.
Correction: Ghazali argues that motion can be the direct requirement of a created meaning, not necessarily a means to an external goal.

What does Ghazali claim the philosophers are forced to accept due to the infinite regress in explaining the sky's distinctive quality?
A) The sky is an animal
B) The existence of a will that specifies
C) The motion is for seeking a place
D) The sky has no motion

Key Takeaway
Ghazali's argument shows that positing natural attributes to explain celestial motion leads to an infinite regress, making a will necessary, and that motion need not be purposive.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you, in your own life, ever assume that everything can be explained by natural causes, avoiding the need for divine will? Think of one area—your health, your provision, your decisions—where you have automatically attributed the outcome to 'how things work' rather than to Allah's will. What would change if you consciously affirmed His will in that matter starting today?
?
The philosophers were certain the sky must be an animal because they could not conceive of motion without purpose. Where are you clinging to certainty about something you have never truly examined? What assumption—perhaps about your own capabilities, or about how the world 'must' work—are you defending without evidence? Are you willing to question it as Ghazali did?
?
Ghazali shows that infinite regress forces one to admit a will that distinguishes. Reflect on a pattern of sin or excuse in your life that you keep explaining away with another excuse, leading to an endless chain. Are you willing to cut the chain by turning to Allah's will to change you, rather than endlessly rationalizing your behavior?

The Philosophers' Argument: Heavenly Motion as Obedience
Ghazali presents the philosophers' argument that heavenly motion is an act of obedience and begins defining 'drawing near'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' argument that heavenly motion is a voluntary act of obedience and drawing near to God, and why they reject spatial and emotional interpretations of drawing near.

Core Idea
Heavenly motion is purposeful obedience, not an arbitrary movement.

Before we can refute the philosophers' claim about heavenly motion, we need to understand their argument in full. They begin by asserting that the heaven's motion is an act of obedience and a means of drawing near to God. But what does 'drawing near' mean when God is not a body in a place? This passage clarifies their foundational concepts.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice how the philosophers establish that every voluntary motion has a purpose.
The argument is built on the principle of 'preference' – why act if inaction is equally possible?
2
Identify the core claim
What is the philosophers' main claim about heavenly motion? They say it is obedience and drawing near. Identify the reasoning linking voluntary motion to purpose.
3
Understand the clarification of 'drawing near'
The philosophers reject two interpretations of drawing near: spatial proximity and emotional closeness. Why? Think about God's transcendence.
This sets the stage for a third meaning they will introduce in the next lesson.

إلى طلب القرب منه في الصفات ، وذلك للآدمى ، بأن يعلم حقائق الأشياء ، وبأن يبقى بعد موته بقاء مؤبداً ، على أكمل أحواله الممكنة له ، فإن البقاء على الكمال الأقصى هو الله تعالى ، والملائكة المقربون كل ما يمكن لهم من الكمال فهو حاضر معهم فى الوجود ، إذ ليس فيهم شيء بالقوة حتى يخرج إلى الفعل . فإذن كما لهم فى الغاية القصوى ، بالإضافة إلى ما سوى الله تعالى . والملائكة السماوية ، هي عبارة عن النفوس المحركة للسموات ، وفيها (1) ما هو بالقوة ، وكمالاتها منقسمة ، إلى ما هو بالفعل ، كالشكل الكرى والهيأة ، وذلك حاضر ، وإلى ما هو بالقوة ، وهو الهيئة في الوضع ، والأين ، وما من وضع معين إلا وهو ممكن له ، ولكن ليس له سائر الأوضاع بالفعل ، فإن الجمع بين جميعها غير ممكن . فلما لم يمكنها استيفاء آحاد الأوضاع على الدوام ، قصدت استيفاءها بالنوع ، فلا يزال يطلب وضعاً بعد وضع وأيناً بعد أين ، ولا ينقطع قط هذا الإمكان ، فلا تنقطع هذه الحركة ، وإنما قصده التشبه بالمبدأ الأول في نيل الكمال الأقصى على حسب الإمكان في حقه ، وهو معنى طاعة الملائكة السماوية لله تعالى الأول . وقد حصل لها التشبه من وجهين : أحدهما : Question [15]: On refuting what they mentioned of the purpose that moves the heaven. And they have said: The heaven is obedient to God, the Exalted, by its motion, and drawing near to Him, because every motion with will is for a purpose, since it is inconceivable that an act and motion should issue from a living being unless the act is more appropriate for it than omission; otherwise, if act and omission were equal, the act would be inconceivable. Then drawing near to God, the Exalted, does not mean seeking pleasure and fearing displeasure, for God, the Exalted, is transcendent above displeasure and pleasure; and if these expressions are used, it is figuratively, metonymically, for willing punishment and willing reward. It is not permissible that drawing near should be by seeking nearness to Him in place, for that is impossible. So nothing remains except seeking nearness to Him in attributes. For the most perfect existence is His existence, and every existence in relation to His existence is deficient, and deficiency has degrees and variation. So the angel is nearer to Him in attribute, not in place, and this is what is meant by the 'near angels'—I mean the intellectual substances that do not change, do not perish, do not undergo transformation, and know things as they are. And the human being, whenever he increases in nearness to the angels in attributes, increases in nearness to God, the Exalted; and the utmost limit of the human level is imitation of the angels. And if it is established that this is the meaning of drawing near to God, the Exalted, and that it reduces to seeking nearness to Him in attributes, and that for the human being it is by knowing the realities of things and by remaining after his death an eternal remaining in the most perfect state possible for him—for remaining in the utmost perfection is God, the Exalted, and the near angels have all the perfection possible for them present with them in existence, since there is nothing in them in potentiality that should come into actuality—then just as they have the utmost limit in relation to other than God, the Exalted... And the heavenly angels are expressions for the souls that move the heavens, and in them there is what is in potentiality, and their perfections are divided into what is in actuality, like the spherical shape and form, and that is present; and into what is in potentiality, which is the state in position and location; and no specific position is possible for it except that it is possible for it, but it does not have all the other positions in actuality, for combining all of them is not possible. So when it is not possible for it to exhaust individual positions perpetually, it intends to exhaust them by species. So it does not cease to seek position after position and location after location, and this possibility never ceases, so this motion does not cease. Its intention is only imitation of the First Principle in attaining the utmost perfection according to what is possible for it, and that is the meaning of the obedience of the heavenly angels to God, the First. And it obtains imitation in two aspects: one of them: exhausting every position possible for it by species, and that is the intent by the primary intent. And the second: what results from its motion of variation of aspects in triangulation, quadrature, conjunction, opposition, and variation of ascendants relative to the earth; then goodness flows from it upon what is beneath the sphere of the moon, and all these occurrences arise from it. So this is the aspect of perfection of the heavenly soul. And every rational soul is desirous of perfection by itself. And the objection to this is that in the premises of this discourse there is that which can be disputed, but we will not prolong it. And we return to the purpose which you ultimately specified and we refute it in two aspects: one of them: that seeking perfection by being in every location that is possible for it is not obedience; and this is nothing but like a person who has no occupation and whose sustenance for his desires and needs is sufficiently provided, so he stands and wanders in a land or house, claiming that he is drawing near.
Source pages p. 222

Commentary
This passage presents the philosophers' argument for the purpose of heavenly motion and Ghazali's initial refutation. The passage begins by stating the issue: refuting the philosophers' claim about the purpose that moves the heaven. The philosophers argue that the heaven is obedient to God through its motion, and this motion is an act of drawing near to God. They reason that any voluntary motion must have a purpose, otherwise the act would be equal to omission and thus inconceivable. Then they clarify that drawing near to God does not mean seeking His pleasure or avoiding His displeasure, as God is transcendent above such emotions; rather, these terms are figurative for reward and punishment. Nor can it mean spatial nearness, as that is impossible. Therefore, drawing near must be seeking nearness in attributes. Since God's existence is the most perfect, all other existences are deficient in varying degrees. Angels are nearer to God in attributes, not place, and are intellectual substances that are unchanging and know things as they are. Humans become nearer to God by becoming like the angels in attributes, and the ultimate human goal is imitation of the angels. Thus, drawing near to God means seeking nearness in attributes, attained by humans through knowing the realities of things and through eternal survival after death in the most perfect possible state. The angels already possess all possible perfection in actuality, with no potentiality. The heavenly angels, which are the souls moving the heavens, have some potentiality—they have actual spherical shape and form, but their position and location are potential, as they cannot occupy all positions simultaneously. Hence, they intend to exhaust positions by species, seeking position after position endlessly, imitating the First Principle in attaining utmost perfection. This imitation has two aspects: first, exhausting every possible position by species; second, the resulting variations in celestial aspects (triangulation, quadrature, etc.) that cause goodness to flow to the sublunar world. Every rational soul desires perfection by itself. Then Ghazali begins his objection, noting that the premises can be disputed. He returns to the specific purpose stated—namely, that the heavenly soul seeks perfection by being in every possible location—and refutes it in two ways, presenting the first: such seeking is not obedience but resembles a person who has no needs and wanders aimlessly, claiming to draw near. The argument is cut off as the window ends.

Key Terms
التقرب (drawing near)
المشيئة (will)
الغرض (purpose)
السخط (displeasure)
الرضى (pleasure)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Drawing near to God means spatial proximity or seeking to please an emotional God.
Correction: The philosophers explicitly deny both, asserting that God is transcendent and not subject to emotions or locations. Drawing near is about attributes.

Checkpoint
According to the philosophers in this passage, what is the primary reason that heavenly motion must have a purpose?
A) Because the heaven is alive and conscious
B) Because every voluntary act requires that the act be preferable to omission
C) Because God commands the heaven to move
D) Because motion is inherent to celestial bodies

Key Takeaway
The philosophers ground heavenly motion in voluntary choice, which requires a purpose, and they define that purpose as drawing near to God by seeking resemblance in attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
What movement in your life is truly motivated by seeking closeness to God—not just to avoid punishment or gain reward, but to resemble His attributes? If none, why not?
?
The philosophers say drawing near is not spatial—so where is your heart? Are you trying to 'get closer' to God by performing rituals in specific places while your character remains unchanged?
?
Examine one action you performed today: was it truly voluntary, done because it was more appropriate than its omission, or was it done out of habit? How does your answer reflect your pursuit of nearness to God?

Drawing Near Through Attributes: The Angelic Model
Ghazali continues presenting the philosophers' view on drawing near as attribute imitation, with angels as the model.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' concept of 'drawing near to God' as seeking nearness in attributes, with angels as the perfect models of this nearness.

Core Idea
Angels are closer to God in attributes, not in place.

Having established that drawing near to God cannot be spatial or emotional, the philosophers now explain the positive meaning: seeking nearness in attributes. They use angels as the paradigm of such nearness and show how humans can imitate them.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the key definition
Look for the statement that 'nothing remains except seeking nearness to Him in attributes.' This is the core definition.
2
Understand the angelic nature
The passage describes angels as 'intellectual substances' that are unchanging, imperishable, and know things as they are. Why are they considered nearer to God in attributes?
Their perfection is in actuality, not potentiality.
3
Trace the human's path to nearness
Humans become near to God by imitating the angels. How? By knowing the realities of things and by eternal survival after death in a perfect state.
This imitation is the ultimate goal of human life.

الألفاظ ، فعلى سبيل المجاز ، يكنى بها ، عن إرادة العقاب ، وإرادة الثواب . ولا يجوز أن يكون التقرب ، بطلب القرب منه في المكان ، فإنه محال . فلا يبقى إلا طلب القرب منه فى الصفات ، فإن الوجود الأكمل وجوده ، وكل وجود فبالإضافة إلى وجوده ناقص ، وللنقصان درجات وتفاوت ، فالملك أقرب إليه صفة لا مكاناً ، وهو المراد بالملائكة المقربين أعنى الجواهر العقلية ، التي لا تتغير ، ولا تفنى ، ولا تستحيل ، وتعلم الأشياء على ما هي عليه ، والإنسان كلما ازداد قرباً من الملائكة فى الصفات ، ازداد قرباً من الله تعالى ، ومنتهى طبقة الآدميين التشبه بالملائكة . وإذا ثبت أن هذا معنى التقرب إلى الله تعالى ، وأنه يرجع إلى طلب القرب منه في الصفات ، وذلك للآدمى ، بأن يعلم حقائق الأشياء ، وبأن يبقى بعد موته بقاء مؤبداً ، على أكمل أحواله الممكنة له ، فإن البقاء على الكمال الأقصى هو الله تعالى ، والملائكة المقربون كل ما يمكن لهم من الكمال فهو حاضر معهم فى الوجود ، إذ ليس فيهم شيء بالقوة حتى يخرج إلى الفعل . فإذن كما لهم فى الغاية القصوى ، بالإضافة إلى ما سوى الله تعالى . والملائكة السماوية ، هي عبارة عن النفوس المحركة للسموات ، وفيها (1) ما هو بالقوة ، وكمالاتها منقسمة ، إلى ما هو بالفعل ، كالشكل الكرى والهيأة ، وذلك حاضر ، وإلى ما هو بالقوة ، وهو الهيئة في الوضع ، والأين ، وما من وضع معين إلا وهو ممكن له ، ولكن ليس له سائر الأوضاع بالفعل ، فإن الجمع بين جميعها غير ممكن . فلما لم يمكنها استيفاء آحاد الأوضاع على الدوام ، قصدت استيفاءها بالنوع ، فلا يزال يطلب وضعاً بعد وضع وأيناً بعد أين ، ولا ينقطع قط هذا الإمكان ، فلا تنقطع هذه الحركة ، وإنما قصده التشبه بالمبدأ الأول في نيل الكمال الأقصى على حسب الإمكان في حقه ، وهو معنى طاعة الملائكة السماوية لله تعالى الأول . وقد حصل لها التشبه من وجهين : أحدهما : استيفاء كل وضع ممكن له بالنوع ، وهو المقصود بالقصد الأول . والثاني : ما يترتب على حركته من اختلاف النسب ، في التثليث والتربيع والمقارنة والمقابلة واختلاف الطوالع بالنسبة إلى الأرض ؛ فيفيض منه الخير على ما تحت فلك القمر ويحصل منه هذه الحوادث كلها فهذا وجه استكمال النفس السماوية . وكل نفس عاقلة فمتشوقة إلى الاستكمال بذاتها . والاعتراض على هذا هو أن في مقدمات هذا الكلام ما يمكن النزاع به ولكنا لا نطول به ونعود إلى الغرض الذي عينتموه آخراً ونبطله من وجهين : أحدهما : أن طلب الاستكمال بالكون في كل أين يمكن أن يكون له لا طاعة وما هذا إلا كإنسان لم يكن له شغل وقد كفى المئونة في شهواته وحاجاته فقام وهو يدور في بلد أو بيت ويزعم أنه يتقرب .
Question [15]: On refuting what they mentioned of the purpose that moves the heaven. And they have said: The heaven is obedient to God, the Exalted, by its motion, and drawing near to Him, because every motion with will is for a purpose, since it is inconceivable that an act and motion should issue from a living being unless the act is more appropriate for it than omission; otherwise, if act and omission were equal, the act would be inconceivable. Then drawing near to God, the Exalted, does not mean seeking pleasure and fearing displeasure, for God, the Exalted, is transcendent above displeasure and pleasure; and if these expressions are used, it is figuratively, metonymically, for willing punishment and willing reward. It is not permissible that drawing near should be by seeking nearness to Him in place, for that is impossible. So nothing remains except seeking nearness to Him in attributes. For the most perfect existence is His existence, and every existence in relation to His existence is deficient, and deficiency has degrees and variation. So the angel is nearer to Him in attribute, not in place, and this is what is meant by the 'near angels'—I mean the intellectual substances that do not change, do not perish, do not undergo transformation, and know things as they are. And the human being, whenever he increases in nearness to the angels in attributes, increases in nearness to God, the Exalted; and the utmost limit of the human level is imitation of the angels. And if it is established that this is the meaning of drawing near to God, the Exalted, and that it reduces to seeking nearness to Him in attributes, and that for the human being it is by knowing the realities of things and by remaining after his death an eternal remaining in the most perfect state possible for him—for remaining in the utmost perfection is God, the Exalted, and the near angels have all the perfection possible for them present with them in existence, since there is nothing in them in potentiality that should come into actuality—then just as they have the utmost limit in relation to other than God, the Exalted... And the heavenly angels are expressions for the souls that move the heavens, and in them there is what is in potentiality, and their perfections are divided into what is in actuality, like the spherical shape and form, and that is present; and into what is in potentiality, which is the state in position and location; and no specific position is possible for it except that it is possible for it, but it does not have all the other positions in actuality, for combining all of them is not possible. So when it is not possible for it to exhaust individual positions perpetually, it intends to exhaust them by species. So it does not cease to seek position after position and location after location, and this possibility never ceases, so this motion does not cease. Its intention is only imitation of the First Principle in attaining the utmost perfection according to what is possible for it, and that is the meaning of the obedience of the heavenly angels to God, the First. And it obtains imitation in two aspects: one of them: exhausting every position possible for it by species, and that is the intent by the primary intent. And the second: what results from its motion of variation of aspects in triangulation, quadrature, conjunction, opposition, and variation of ascendants relative to the earth; then goodness flows from it upon what is beneath the sphere of the moon, and all these occurrences arise from it. So this is the aspect of perfection of the heavenly soul. And every rational soul is desirous of perfection by itself. And the objection to this is that in the premises of this discourse there is that which can be disputed, but we will not prolong it. And we return to the purpose which you ultimately specified and we refute it in two aspects: one of them: that seeking perfection by being in every location that is possible for it is not obedience; and this is nothing but like a person who has no occupation and whose sustenance for his desires and needs is sufficiently provided, so he stands and wanders in a land or house, claiming that he is drawing near.
Source pages p. 222

Commentary
This passage presents the philosophers' argument for the purpose of heavenly motion and Ghazali's initial refutation. The passage begins by stating the issue: refuting the philosophers' claim about the purpose that moves the heaven. The philosophers argue that the heaven is obedient to God through its motion, and this motion is an act of drawing near to God. They reason that any voluntary motion must have a purpose, otherwise the act would be equal to omission and thus inconceivable. Then they clarify that drawing near to God does not mean seeking His pleasure or avoiding His displeasure, as God is transcendent above such emotions; rather, these terms are figurative for reward and punishment. Nor can it mean spatial nearness, as that is impossible. Therefore, drawing near must be seeking nearness in attributes. Since God's existence is the most perfect, all other existences are deficient in varying degrees. Angels are nearer to God in attributes, not place, and are intellectual substances that are unchanging and know things as they are. Humans become nearer to God by becoming like the angels in attributes, and the ultimate human goal is imitation of the angels. Thus, drawing near to God means seeking nearness in attributes, attained by humans through knowing the realities of things and through eternal survival after death in the most perfect possible state. The angels already possess all possible perfection in actuality, with no potentiality. The heavenly angels, which are the souls moving the heavens, have some potentiality—they have actual spherical shape and form, but their position and location are potential, as they cannot occupy all positions simultaneously. Hence, they intend to exhaust positions by species, seeking position after position endlessly, imitating the First Principle in attaining utmost perfection. This imitation has two aspects: first, exhausting every possible position by species; second, the resulting variations in celestial aspects (triangulation, quadrature, etc.) that cause goodness to flow to the sublunar world. Every rational soul desires perfection by itself. Then Ghazali begins his objection, noting that the premises can be disputed. He returns to the specific purpose stated—namely, that the heavenly soul seeks perfection by being in every possible location—and refutes it in two ways, presenting the first: such seeking is not obedience but resembles a person who has no needs and wanders aimlessly, claiming to draw near. The argument is cut off as the window ends.

Key Terms
الصفات (attributes)
الجواهر العقلية (intellectual substances)
الملائكة المقربين (near angels)
التشبه (imitation)
البقاء (remaining/eternity)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Angels are closer to God because they live in a heavenly realm spatially nearer to God.
Correction: The philosophers specify that nearness is in attributes, not place. Angels are nearer because they possess greater perfection, such as immutability and complete knowledge.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ultimate goal for human beings in relation to angels?
A) To become angels themselves
B) To imitate the angels in attributes
C) To worship angels as intercessors
D) To live in the same realm as angels

Key Takeaway
Nearness to God means resemblance in attributes, and angels are the perfect exemplars of such nearness because of their immutable, eternal, and knowledgeable nature.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about "drawing near to God," do you secretly imagine a spatial distance, as if God is far away in heaven? How does the philosophers' definition of nearness as attribute imitation challenge your own daily pursuit of closeness to God, and what specific attribute of the angels (immutability, knowledge, or immortality) are you most lacking in right now? Be honest: are you willing to change that this week?
?
The passage says that the human end is to imitate the angels. But look at your life: are you actively trying to become like a fixed, unchanging intellectual substance, or are you drifting with your desires? What would it mean for you to "know things as they are"—not as you wish them to be—and how would that transform your decisions tomorrow?
?
You claim to want nearness to God, but do you actually seek to acquire the attributes that make the angels near? Name one concrete attribute from the passage (e.g., not changing in your commitment, knowing truth, or being free of decay) that you will pursue today. If you don't pursue it, are you being honest about your desire for God?

Causality and Created Things
intermediate
6 min
Heavenly Motion as Imitation and Its First Refutation
Ghazali details the philosophers' account of heavenly motion as imitation and then presents his first refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' explanation of heavenly souls' motion as an imitation of the First Principle through exhausting possible positions, and Ghazali's first refutation that such motion is not obedience but aimless wandering.

Core Idea
Heavenly souls move to imitate divine perfection, but Ghazali calls it aimless.

The philosophers now specify that the heavenly souls (angels moving the spheres) have potentiality in position and location. Their motion is an attempt to exhaust all possible positions, imitating the First Principle's perfection. Ghazali then begins his refutation by arguing that this behavior is not obedient but akin to a person wandering without purpose.

Guided Path
1
Understand the nature of heavenly souls
Heavenly souls have some perfections in actuality (shape) but potentiality in position and location. Why can't they occupy all positions at once?
Because space is finite but positions are many.
2
Grasp the purpose of motion
To exhaust positions by species, not individually, aiming to imitate the First Principle's perfection. Identify the two aspects of imitation.
First: exhausting positions; second: resulting effects on the sublunar world.
3
Analyze Ghazali's first refutation
Ghazali says this seeking of every possible location is not obedience. He compares it to a person who has all needs met and wanders aimlessly. Why is this a powerful critique?
It challenges the very definition of obedience and purpose.

مسألة [١٥] في إبطال ما ذكروه من الغرض المحرك للسماء وقد قالوا : إن السماء مطيعة لله تعالى ، بحركتها ، ومتقربة إليه ، لأن كل حركة بالإرادة ، فهى لغرض ، إذ لا يتصور أن يصدر الفعل والحركة من حيوان ، إلا Commentary
This passage presents the philosophers' argument for the purpose of heavenly motion and Ghazali's initial refutation. The passage begins by stating the issue: refuting the philosophers' claim about the purpose that moves the heaven. The philosophers argue that the heaven is obedient to God through its motion, and this motion is an act of drawing near to God. They reason that any voluntary motion must have a purpose, otherwise the act would be equal to omission and thus inconceivable. Then they clarify that drawing near to God does not mean seeking His pleasure or avoiding His displeasure, as God is transcendent above such emotions; rather, these terms are figurative for reward and punishment. Nor can it mean spatial nearness, as that is impossible. Therefore, drawing near must be seeking nearness in attributes. Since God's existence is the most perfect, all other existences are deficient in varying degrees. Angels are nearer to God in attributes, not place, and are intellectual substances that are unchanging and know things as they are. Humans become nearer to God by becoming like the angels in attributes, and the ultimate human goal is imitation of the angels. Thus, drawing near to God means seeking nearness in attributes, attained by humans through knowing the realities of things and through eternal survival after death in the most perfect possible state. The angels already possess all possible perfection in actuality, with no potentiality. The heavenly angels, which are the souls moving the heavens, have some potentiality—they have actual spherical shape and form, but their position and location are potential, as they cannot occupy all positions simultaneously. Hence, they intend to exhaust positions by species, seeking position after position endlessly, imitating the First Principle in attaining utmost perfection. This imitation has two aspects: first, exhausting every possible position by species; second, the resulting variations in celestial aspects (triangulation, quadrature, etc.) that cause goodness to flow to the sublunar world. Every rational soul desires perfection by itself. Then Ghazali begins his objection, noting that the premises can be disputed. He returns to the specific purpose stated—namely, that the heavenly soul seeks perfection by being in every possible location—and refutes it in two ways, presenting the first: such seeking is not obedience but resembles a person who has no needs and wanders aimlessly, claiming to draw near. The argument is cut off as the window ends.

Key Terms
النفوس المحركة (moving souls)
القوة (potentiality)
الفعل (actuality)
الأين (location)
التشبه (imitation)
المبدأ الأول (First Principle)
الاستكمال (seeking perfection)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Imitating God means trying to do everything possible, including occupying every place.
Correction: Ghazali argues that such behavior is not true imitation or obedience, as it lacks a genuine purpose and resembles aimless wandering.

Checkpoint
Ghazali compares the heavenly soul's motion of seeking every possible location to what?
A) A dancer performing a ritual
B) A person wandering in a country or house after having all needs met
C) A soldier marching in formation
D) A scholar seeking knowledge

Key Takeaway
The philosophers view heavenly motion as imitation of divine perfection, but Ghazali's first refutation denies that seeking every location qualifies as obedient worship.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever engage in religious acts that are merely repetitive motions without true intention—like the heavenly soul's movement that Ghazali calls aimless? What would it take for your worship to be a true seeking of nearness to God rather than a mechanical routine?
?
When you consider your own pursuit of perfection, is it driven by a genuine desire to imitate God's attributes (knowledge, mercy) or is it a restless wandering from one aimless activity to another? How can you distinguish between the two in your daily life?
?
Ghazali compares the philosophers' heavenly motion to a person who has no needs and wanders. In your life, are there areas where you are 'moving' (busy, active) but without real purpose—claiming to draw near while actually just wandering? What one change can you make today to turn aimless motion into true worship?

Causality and Created Things
beginner
5 min
The Philosophers' Theory of Celestial Motion
Exposition of the philosophers' theory of celestial motion as perfection-seeking.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' claim that celestial souls move the heavens to imitate the First Principle by sequentially covering all possible positions, thereby achieving perfection.

Core Idea
Celestial motion is explained as a perpetual quest for perfection through imitation of the divine.

Before Ghazali refutes the philosophers' theory, he presents it fairly. According to them, the celestial angels are conscious souls that move the heavens because they cannot actualize all possible positions at once, so they seek them over time. This movement is an act of obedience and imitation of God, and it also produces beneficial effects in the sublunar world. Understanding this claim is essential to appreciating Ghazali's critique.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how the philosophers describe the celestial souls as having both actual and potential perfections.
2
Identify the two aspects of imitation
The passage says the souls imitate the First Principle in two ways. Find them: one related to covering positions, the other to producing effects in the lower world.
3
Understand the rationale for perpetual motion
Because the souls cannot occupy all positions at once, they move continuously. This is presented as a desire for perfection.
The movement never ceases because the potential for new positions never ends.
4
Reflect on the motive
Why do the philosophers say this motion is obedience? Note the connection to imitating the First Principle.

The celestial angels are expressions for the souls that move the heavens, and in them is that which is in potentiality, and their perfections are divided into what is in actuality like the spherical shape and the figure, and that is present, and into what is in potentiality, which is the state in position and location. No specific position is except that it is possible for it, but it does not have the rest of the positions in actuality, for the combination of all of them is not possible. So when it was not possible for them to fulfill the individual positions perpetually, they intended to fulfill them by kind, so they continue seeking position after position and location after location, and this possibility never ceases, so this movement does not cease. Their intent is only to imitate the First Principle in attaining the utmost perfection according to what is possible for them, and this is the meaning of the obedience of the celestial angels to God Most High. They attained imitation in two aspects: One of them: fulfilling every possible position by kind, which is the intended purpose by the first intention. The second: what results from their movement of the differences in relations in trines, quartiles, conjunctions, oppositions, and the differences of the ascendants relative to the earth; so goodness flows from it upon what is under the sphere of the moon, and all these events occur from it. This is the aspect of the perfection of the celestial soul. And every rational soul is desirous of perfection through its essence. The objection to it, but we do not prolong foolishness. That is that in the premises of this speech there is that which can be disputed. We return to the purpose which you specified lastly, and we refute it from two aspects: One of them: That seeking perfection by being in every location that can be for it is not obedience. This is but like a person who has no occupation and has been provided with his pleasures and needs, so he stands and goes around in a country or a house, claiming that he is drawing near to God Most High, for he seeks perfection by obtaining for himself being in every place possible, and claims that being in places is possible for me, but I am not able to combine them in number, so I fulfill them by kind, for therein is perfection and nearness. Then one would deem him foolish and consider it foolishness, and it is said: moving from one space to another and from one place to another is not something to be regarded or aspired to. And there is no difference between what they mentioned and this. And the second: It is that we say: What you mentioned of the purpose is obtained by the westward movement, was the first movement eastward? Why were not all movements in one direction? If there is a purpose in their difference, would they not have differed in reverse? So that which is eastward would be westward and which is westward would be eastward? For all that you mentioned of the occurrence of events by the difference of movements, such as trines, sextiles, and others, occurs also by its reverse. Similarly what they mentioned of fulfilling positions and locations — how? It is possible for it to move to the other direction, so why does it not move once from one side and once from another side to fulfill what is possible for it, if in fulfilling every possible there is perfection? So it indicates that these are fancies with no substance, and that the secrets of the kingdom of the heavens are not known through such imaginations. Rather God grants knowledge of them to His prophets and saints through inspiration, not through deduction. Therefore the very last of them was unable to explain the reason for the direction of movement and its choice. And some of them said: Since its perfection is obtained by movement from any direction, and the order of terrestrial events requires difference of movements and determination of directions, the motive for the movement itself is drawing near to God, and the motive for the direction of movement is the flow of goodness upon the lower world. This is false from two aspects: One of them: That if that can be imagined, let it be judged that its inherent nature...
Source pages p. 223

Commentary
This passage is an extended refutation by Ghazali of the philosophers' theory that the celestial souls (angels) move the heavens in order to perfect themselves by covering all possible positions, thereby imitating the First Principle. Ghazali first summarizes the philosophers' argument: the celestial souls have potential and actual perfections; they cannot occupy all positions simultaneously, so they seek to fulfill them sequentially by continuous motion, which is an act of obedience and imitation of God. They claim this motion also produces beneficial effects in the sublunar world through astrological configurations. Ghazali then announces he will refute their claimed purpose from two aspects. The first refutation uses an analogy: a man with no necessities goes around aimlessly in a house or country, claiming that being in every possible place is a form of worship and self-perfection. This is clearly absurd — mere spatial movement is not obedience. The philosophers' reasoning, Ghazali argues, is no different; they have not established that such movement has inherent value. The second refutation questions the specific direction of motion. If the purpose is to cover all positions, why not move in both directions (east and west) alternately? The philosophers assume westward motion, but eastward would equally fulfill the claimed purpose. The fact that movements are consistently in one direction suggests something else at play, not the philosophers' fancy. Ghazali concludes that these theories are baseless imaginations; true knowledge of the heavens is given by God to prophets and saints through inspiration, not deduction. He then mentions an alternative explanation from some philosophers that the movement direction is to benefit the lower world, but he starts to refute it as false from two aspects, though the passage cuts off before completing the first.

According to the philosophers, why do celestial souls move continuously?
A) Because they are forced by God
B) Because they cannot occupy all positions at once and seek to fulfill them sequentially
C) Because they want to escape the heavens
D) Because they are attracted by earthly desires

Key Takeaway
The philosophers' theory explains celestial motion as a rational soul's perpetual effort to imitate God by sequentially occupying all possible positions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The celestial souls never cease moving toward perfection because the possibility of new positions never ends. In your own life, do you treat each day as a new position to occupy in imitation of God, or do you settle into a fixed state? What concrete change can you make today to resume that perpetual movement?
?
Every rational soul desires perfection through its essence, but this requires imitating the First Principle. What specific attribute of God (e.g., mercy, justice, knowledge) are you failing to imitate in your daily interactions? What is the first step to embodying that attribute?
?
If the celestial angels' motion is an act of obedience, what does your own 'motion'—your daily actions and pursuits—say about whom or what you are obeying? Are you moving toward God or away from Him? What will you do to reorient your trajectory?

Causality and Created Things

Ghazali's First Refutation: The Absurdity of Aimless Motion
Ghazali's first refutation using an analogy of a walking man.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's first refutation, where he uses an analogy to show that mere spatial movement cannot be considered an act of worship or perfection.

Core Idea
Moving aimlessly through space is not obedience, no matter how many places you cover.

After summarizing the philosophers' position, Ghazali announces his refutation from two aspects. In the first aspect, he compares the celestial souls' supposed pursuit of perfection to a man who walks around a house claiming that being in every possible place is worship. With this analogy, Ghazali intends to show the absurdity of attributing intrinsic value to mere spatial displacement.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and find the analogy
Ghazali presents an analogy of a man walking around a house or country. Read it carefully.
2
Understand the point of the analogy
The man claims that covering all possible places is a form of worship. Ghazali says this is clearly foolish. Why?
The movement itself has no inherent moral or spiritual value.
3
Connect it to the philosophers' claim
Ghazali states that the philosophers' reasoning is no different. The celestial souls moving to cover positions is just as absurd.
4
Note the structure of the refutation
This is the first of two aspects. Ghazali also introduces the second aspect but does not elaborate here.
The second aspect will be in the next lesson.

Commentary
This passage is an extended refutation by Ghazali of the philosophers' theory that the celestial souls (angels) move the heavens in order to perfect themselves by covering all possible positions, thereby imitating the First Principle. This is clearly absurd — mere spatial movement is not obedience. The philosophers' reasoning, Ghazali argues, is no different; they have not established that such movement has inherent value. He then mentions an alternative explanation from some philosophers that the movement direction is to benefit the lower world, but he starts to refute it as false from two aspects, though the passage cuts off before completing the first.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That any kind of movement can be an act of worship if it covers many places.
Correction: Ghazali shows through analogy that aimless spatial movement has no inherent spiritual value; true obedience requires a meaningful purpose.

Checkpoint
What does Ghazali compare the celestial souls' motion to in his first refutation?
A) A bird flying in circles
B) A man walking around a house claiming to worship by covering all places
C) A child playing in the street
D) A merchant traveling for trade

Key Takeaway
Ghazali refutes the philosophers by arguing that seeking perfection through mere spatial motion is no different from aimlessly wandering and cannot be considered obedience.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a religious action you perform regularly—prayer, charity, or even attending a lecture. Is there any part of it that has become mere motion, a habit without conscious intent to obey God? What would it take to return that action to genuine obedience starting tomorrow?
?
The man in the analogy believed his wandering was worship. Where in your life might you be mistaking activity for piety—doing many things but avoiding the one thing God actually requires of you right now? What is one concrete step you can take today to replace aimless motion with purposeful surrender?

Ghazali's Second Refutation: Direction of Motion and the Limits of Reason
Ghazali's second refutation and epistemological conclusion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's second refutation, which questions why celestial motion is only in one direction, and his epistemological conclusion that the secrets of the heavens are known through inspiration, not deduction.

Core Idea
If covering all positions is the goal, why not move both east and west? The inconsistency reveals the theory as fantasy.

In his second refutation, Ghazali argues that if the purpose of celestial motion were to cover all possible positions, then the souls should move in both eastward and westward directions to fulfill all possibilities. But they only move westward, which contradicts the supposed goal. Ghazali then concludes that such theories are baseless, and true knowledge of the heavens comes only through divine inspiration, not human deduction. He also begins to address an alternative explanation, though the passage is cut off.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the key question
Ghazali asks: why is the movement only westward, and not also eastward? This is the core of his second refutation.
2
Understand the implication
If the claim is that motion seeks to cover all possible positions, then moving in only one direction fails to achieve that. The philosophers have not justified the direction.
3
Note the epistemological conclusion
Ghazali states that such theories are fancies; the true secrets of the heavens are known only through inspiration (ilham) given to prophets and saints, not deduction (istidlal).
4
Consider the alternative explanation
Some philosophers suggested that the direction is determined by the need to regulate terrestrial events. Ghazali begins to refute this but the passage is cut off. Reflect on how this connects to his overall critique.

والملائكة السماوية، هي عبارة عن النفوس المحركة للسموات، وفيها ما هو بالقوة، وكمالاتها منقسمة إلى ما هو بالفعل كالشكل الكري والهيأة وذلك حاضر، وإلى ما هو بالقوة وهو الهيئة في الوضع والأين، وما من وضع معين إلا وهو ممكن له، ولكن ليس له سائر الأوضاع بالفعل، فإن الجمع بين جميعها غير ممكن. فلما لم يمكنها استيفاء آحاد الأوضاع على الدوام، قصدت استيفاءها بالنوع، فلا يزال يطلب وضعاً بعد وضع وأيناً بعد أين، ولا ينقطع قط هذا الإمكان، فلا تنقطع هذه الحركة، وإنما قصده التشبه بالمبدأ الأول في نيل الكمال الأقصى على حسب الإمكان في حقه، وهو معنى طاعة الملائكة السماوية لله تعالى. وقد حصل لها التشبه من وجهين: أحدهما: استيفاء كل وضع ممكن له بالنوع، وهو المقصود بالقصد الأول. والثاني: ما يترتب على حركته من اختلاف النسب في التثليث والتربيع والمقارنة والمقابلة واختلاف الطوالع بالنسبة إلى الأرض؛ فيفيض منه الخير على ما تحت فلك القمر، ويحصل منه هذه الحوادث كلها، فهذا وجه استكمال النفس السماوية. وكل نفس عاقلة فمتشوقة إلى الاستكمال بذاتها. والاعتراض على فيه، ولكنا لا نطول حماقة. هذا هو أن في مقدمات هذا الكلام ما يمكن النزاع به. ونعود إلى الغرض الذي عينتموه آخراً، ونبطله من وجهين: أحدهما: أن طلب الاستكمال بالكون في كل أين يمكن أن يكون له، لا طاعة، وما هذا إلا كإنسان لم يكن له شغل، وقد كفى المئونة في شهواته وحاجاته، فقام وهو يدور في بلد أو بيت، ويزعم أنه يتقرب إلى الله تعالى، فإنه يستكمل بأن يحصل لنفسه الكون في كل مكان أمكن، وزعم أن الكون في الأماكن ممكن لي، ولست أقدر على الجمع بينها بالعدد، فأستوفيه بالنوع، فإن فيه استكمالاً وتقرباً، فيسفه عقله فيه ويحمل على الحماقة، ويقال: الانتقال من حيز إلى حيز ومن مكان إلى مكان ليس كما لا يعتد به أو يتشوف إليه، ولا فرق بين ما ذكروه وبين هذا. والثاني: هو أنا نقول: ما ذكرتموه من الغرض حاصل بالحركة المغربية كانت الحركة الأولى مشرقية؟! وهلا كانت حركات الكل إلى جهة واحدة؟! فإن كان في اختلافها غرض، فهلا اختلفت بالعكس؟! فكانت التي هي مشرقية مغربية والتي هي مغربية مشرقية؟! فإن كل ما ذكرتموه من حصول الحوادث باختلاف الحركات من التثليثات والتسديسات وغيرها يحصل بعكسه، وكذا ما ذكروه من استيفاء الأوضاع والأين، كيف؟! ومن الممكن لها الحركة إلى الجهة الأخرى، فما بالها لا تتحرك مرة من جانب ومرة من جانب استيفاء لما يمكن لها إن كان في استيفاء كل ممكن كمال. فدل أن هذه خيالات لا حاصل لها، وأن أسرار ملكوت السموات لا يطلع عليها بأمثال هذه التخيلات، وإنما يتطلع الله عليها أنبياءه وأولياءه على سبيل الإلهام لا على سبيل الاستدلال، ولذلك عجز من عند آخرهم عن بيان السبب في جهة الحركة واختيارها. وقال بعضهم: لما كان استكمالها يحصل بالحركة من أى جهة كانت، وكان انتظام الحوادث الأرضية يستدعى اختلاف حركات وتعيين جهات، كان الداعى لها إلى أصل الحركة التقرب إلى الله تعالى، والداعي إلى جهة الحركة إفاضة الخير على العالم السفلى. وهذا باطل، من وجهين: أحدهما: أن ذلك إن أمكن أن يتخيل، فليقض بأن مقتضى طبعه
The celestial angels are expressions for the souls that move the heavens, and in them is that which is in potentiality, and their perfections are divided into what is in actuality like the spherical shape and the figure, and that is present, and into what is in potentiality, which is the state in position and location. No specific position is except that it is possible for it, but it does not have the rest of the positions in actuality, for the combination of all of them is not possible. So when it was not possible for them to fulfill the individual positions perpetually, they intended to fulfill them by kind, so they continue seeking position after position and location after location, and this possibility never ceases, so this movement does not cease. Their intent is only to imitate the First Principle in attaining the utmost perfection according to what is possible for them, and this is the meaning of the obedience of the celestial angels to God Most High. They attained imitation in two aspects: One of them: fulfilling every possible position by kind, which is the intended purpose by the first intention. The second: what results from their movement of the differences in relations in trines, quartiles, conjunctions, oppositions, and the differences of the ascendants relative to the earth; so goodness flows from it upon what is under the sphere of the moon, and all these events occur from it. This is the aspect of the perfection of the celestial soul. And every rational soul is desirous of perfection through its essence. The objection to it, but we do not prolong foolishness. That is that in the premises of this speech there is that which can be disputed. We return to the purpose which you specified lastly, and we refute it from two aspects: One of them: That seeking perfection by being in every location that can be for it is not obedience. This is but like a person who has no occupation and has been provided with his pleasures and needs, so he stands and goes around in a country or a house, claiming that he is drawing near to God Most High, for he seeks perfection by obtaining for himself being in every place possible, and claims that being in places is possible for me, but I am not able to combine them in number, so I fulfill them by kind, for therein is perfection and nearness. Then one would deem him foolish and consider it foolishness, and it is said: moving from one space to another and from one place to another is not something to be regarded or aspired to. And there is no difference between what they mentioned and this. And the second: It is that we say: What you mentioned of the purpose is obtained by the westward movement, was the first movement eastward? Why were not all movements in one direction? If there is a purpose in their difference, would they not have differed in reverse? So that which is eastward would be westward and which is westward would be eastward? For all that you mentioned of the occurrence of events by the difference of movements, such as trines, sextiles, and others, occurs also by its reverse. Similarly what they mentioned of fulfilling positions and locations — how? It is possible for it to move to the other direction, so why does it not move once from one side and once from another side to fulfill what is possible for it, if in fulfilling every possible there is perfection? So it indicates that these are fancies with no substance, and that the secrets of the kingdom of the heavens are not known through such imaginations. Rather God grants knowledge of them to His prophets and saints through inspiration, not through deduction. Therefore the very last of them was unable to explain the reason for the direction of movement and its choice. And some of them said: Since its perfection is obtained by movement from any direction, and the order of terrestrial events requires difference of movements and determination of directions, the motive for the movement itself is drawing near to God, and the motive for the direction of movement is the flow of goodness upon the lower world. This is false from two aspects: One of them: That if that can be imagined, let it be judged that its inherent nature...
Source pages p. 223

Commentary
This passage is an extended refutation by Ghazali of the philosophers' theory that the celestial souls (angels) move the heavens in order to perfect themselves by covering all possible positions, thereby imitating the First Principle. Ghazali first summarizes the philosophers' argument: the celestial souls have potential and actual perfections; they cannot occupy all positions simultaneously, so they seek to fulfill them sequentially by continuous motion, which is an act of obedience and imitation of God. They claim this motion also produces beneficial effects in the sublunar world through astrological configurations. Ghazali then announces he will refute their claimed purpose from two aspects. The first refutation uses an analogy: a man with no necessities goes around aimlessly in a house or country, claiming that being in every possible place is a form of worship and self-perfection. This is clearly absurd — mere spatial movement is not obedience. The philosophers' reasoning, Ghazali argues, is no different; they have not established that such movement has inherent value. The second refutation questions the specific direction of motion. If the purpose is to cover all positions, why not move in both directions (east and west) alternately? The philosophers assume westward motion, but eastward would equally fulfill the claimed purpose. The fact that movements are consistently in one direction suggests something else at play, not the philosophers' fancy. Ghazali concludes that these theories are baseless imaginations; true knowledge of the heavens is given by God to prophets and saints through inspiration, not deduction. He then mentions an alternative explanation from some philosophers that the movement direction is to benefit the lower world, but he starts to refute it as false from two aspects, though the passage cuts off before completing the first.

Mocking the Perfection Through Motion Argument
Ghazali mocks the philosophers' perfection-through-motion claim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's first refutation of the philosophers' claim that celestial motion is a form of self-perfection and closeness to God.

Core Idea
Claiming movement between places is perfection is as foolish as aimless wandering.

Philosophers argued that celestial bodies move to perfect themselves and draw nearer to God. Ghazali begins his refutation by reducing this idea to absurdity: if moving from place to place constitutes perfection, then a person who wanders aimlessly is also perfecting himself. This lesson presents that first mockery.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice how Ghazali uses sarcasm to attack the philosophers' reasoning.
The passage is a single extended analogy.
2
Identify the target claim
What exactly are the philosophers claiming about celestial motion? Identify the idea that motion is for 'perfection and closeness' to God.
3
Analyze the mockery
How does Ghazali turn the philosophers' argument into something ridiculous? He compares it to a person moving aimlessly and calling it perfection.
Think about the strength of this rhetorical move.
4
Reflect on the implication
Why is this a powerful refutation? It shows the philosophers' reasoning is not logically necessary and can be parodied.
This connects to the broader theme of the limits of reason.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إلى الله تعالى ، فإنه يستكمل ، بأن يحصل لنفسه ، الكون في كل مكان أمكن ، وزعم أن الكون فى الأماكن ممكن لى ، ولست أقدر على الجمع بينها بالعدد ، فأستوفيه بالنوع ، فإن فيه استكمالاً وتقرباً ، فيسفه عقله فيه و يحمل على الحماقة ، ويقال : الانتقال من حيز ، إلى حيز ، ومن مكان إلى مكان ، ليس كما لا يعتد به ، أو يتشوف إليه ، ولا فرق بين ما ذكروه ، وبين هذا. والثانى ، هو أنا نقول : ما ذكرتموه من الغرض، حاصل بالحركة المغربية كانت الحركة الأولى مشرقية ؟ ! وهلا كانت حركات الكل إلى جهة واحدة ! ! فإن كان في اختلافها غرض ، فهلا اختلفت با لعكس !! فكانت التي هي مشرقية مغربية ، والتي هي مغربية مشرقية ؛ فإن كل ما ذكرتموه من حصول الحوادث ، باختلاف الحركات ، من التثليثات والتسديسات وغيرها، يحصل بعكسه ، وكذا ما ذكروه من استيفاء الأوضاع والإيون ، كيف ! ! ومن الممكن لها الحركة إلى الجهة الأخرى ، فما بالها لا تتحرك مرة من جانب ، ومرة من جانب ، استيفاء لما يمكن لها إن كان في استيفاء كل ممكن كمال. فدل أن هذه خيالات لا حاصل لها ، وأن أسرار ملكوت السموات ، لا يطلع عليها ، بأمثال هذه التخيلات، وإنما يتطلع الله عليها ، أنبياءه وأولياءه ، على سبيل الإلهام، لا على سبيل الاستدلال ، ولذلك عجز من عند آخرهم ، عن بيان السبب في جهة الحركة واختيارها.
To God Almighty, for he is perfected by obtaining for himself existence in every possible place. And he claimed that existence in places is possible for me, and I cannot combine them by number, so I fulfill it by type, for therein is perfection and closeness. So one becomes foolish in his intellect and bears foolishness, and it is said: Transition from a space to a space, and from a place to a place, is not something to be considered or aspired to. And there is no difference between what they mentioned and this. And the second is that we say: The purpose you mentioned is obtained by the western motion, even if the first motion was eastern? Why were not all motions towards one direction? For if there is a purpose in their difference, why were they not reversed? So that what is eastern becomes western, and what is western becomes eastern. For everything you mentioned of the occurrence of events by the difference of motions, such as trines, sextiles, and others, occurs by its opposite, as well as what they mentioned of exhausting positions and aspects. How? It is possible for it to move to the other direction. So why does it not move sometimes from one side and sometimes from another, exhausting what is possible for it, if there is perfection in exhausting every possibility. So this indicates that these are fanciful notions with no substance, and that the secrets of the kingdom of the heavens are not disclosed by such fancies; rather, God discloses them to His prophets and saints by way of inspiration, not by way of demonstration. Therefore, the last of them was unable to explain the cause of the direction of motion and its choice.
Source pages p. 224

Commentary
This passage is part of Ghazali's refutation of the philosophers' claim that celestial bodies move to perfect themselves and draw closer to God. Ghazali first mocks the idea that movement from place to place constitutes perfection, comparing it to a fool moving aimlessly. He then presents a second refutation: he argues that if the purpose of motion is to produce events (like astrological aspects), then the specific directions (eastern vs. western) are arbitrary. The same effects could be achieved by opposite motions. Therefore, the philosophers' explanations are mere fancies. Ghazali concludes that the true secrets of the heavens are known only through divine inspiration to prophets and saints, not through such speculative reasoning. This underscores the limits of human reason in grasping celestial realities.

تقرب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the physical movement of celestial bodies from place to place is a rational means of perfection and closeness to God.
Correction: Ghazali shows that such movement, if applied to humans, is aimless and foolish, so it cannot be a valid explanation for celestial motion.

Checkpoint
What does Ghazali compare the philosophers' argument about celestial motion to?
A) A person praying in every mosque
B) A person moving aimlessly from place to place claiming perfection
C) A bird flying to different nests
D) A planet orbiting the sun

Key Takeaway
If moving between places were perfection, then aimless wandering would be divine worship — a clear absurdity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever measured your spiritual closeness to God by how many religious activities you do or places you visit? How does Ghazali's mockery challenge that assumption in your own life? What would it take to shift your focus from quantity to quality?
?
When you find yourself constantly seeking new experiences or 'next steps' in your faith journey, do you stop to ask whether they truly bring you closer to God or are just aimless motion? This lesson warns that movement alone is not perfection. Where in your life might you be confusing busyness with growth?
?
This lesson shows how a seemingly sophisticated argument can collapse when you follow it to its logical conclusion. Are there beliefs or habits you hold that crumble under the same test? Will you honestly examine one today, even if it makes you uncomfortable?

Arbitrariness of Direction and Divine Inspiration
Ghazali argues that celestial directions are arbitrary and the cause is known only through inspiration.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's second refutation: that the specific directions of celestial motions are arbitrary and the true cause is known only through divine inspiration.

Core Idea
If the purpose of motion is to produce events, reversing directions would produce the same effects.

Ghazali continues his critique by questioning why celestial motions occur in specific directions (eastern vs western). He argues that the same astrological events could be achieved if the directions were reversed, showing that the philosophers' reasoning is not necessary. He concludes that the real secrets of the heavens are known only through inspiration to prophets and saints.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. This passage contains two sub-arguments: (a) why not all motions in one direction? (b) why not reverse directions?
Notice the rhetorical questions.
2
Analyze the first question: uniformity of direction
Ghazali asks: if the purpose (producing events) is achieved by westward motion, why not let all motions be westward? Why do they vary?
This challenges the necessity of multiple directions.
3
Analyze the second question: reversal of directions
Why are the motions not reversed? Since the same events (trines, sextiles) can occur with opposite motions, the specific direction is irrelevant.
4
Understand the conclusion
Ghazali declares that such arguments are mere 'fanciful notions' and that true celestial secrets are revealed by God to His chosen ones through inspiration, not human reasoning.
This is a key statement on epistemology.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Ghazali's Refutation of Celestial Direction
Ghazali refutes two aspects of the philosophers' rejoinder on celestial motion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's two refutations of the philosophers' argument that celestial motion has a specific direction for benefiting the lower world.

Core Idea
The specific direction of celestial motion is arbitrary and not rationally necessary.

Philosophers argued that celestial bodies move to draw near to God and that their specific directions are for ordering sublunary events. Ghazali counters by showing that one could equally imagine rest as the natural state, and that any set of opposite directions would produce the required differences. This lesson unpacks his two-fold critique.

Guided Path
1
Read the philosophers' rejoinder
Understand the claim: motion is for perfection (nearness to God) and specific direction is for benefiting the lower world.
This sets up the target of Ghazali's refutation.
2
First refutation: rest as alternative
Ghazali argues that if motion can be imagined for benefiting others, then rest (imitating God's transcendence) is equally imaginable. The choice is arbitrary.
Notice the symmetry: if motion is possible, rest is also possible.
3
Second refutation: specific direction unnecessary
Ghazali shows that only difference of directions is needed, not a particular direction. Any opposite pair suffices, so the philosophers' specific assignment is baseless.
Focus on the point that requiring only difference leaves direction arbitrary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال بعضهم : لما كان استكمالها يحصل بالحركة ، من أى جهة كانت ، وكان انتظام الحوادث الأرضية ، يستدعى اختلاف حركات ، وتعيين جهات ، كان الداعى لها إلى أصل الحركة ، التقرب إلى الله تعالى ، والداعي إلى جهة الحركة ، إفاضة الخير على العالم السفلى . وهذا باطل ، من وجهين : أحدهما : أن ذلك إن أمكن أن يتخيل ، فليقض بأن مقتضى طبعه السكون ، احترازاً عن الحركة والتغير ، وهذا تشبه بالله تعالى على التحقيق ، فإنه مقدس عن التغير ، والحركة تغير ، ولكنه اختار الحركة لإفاضة الخير ، فإنه كان ينتفع به غيره ، وليس يثقل عليه الحركة ، وليست تتعبه ، فما المانع من هذا الخيال ؟ ! والثانى : أن الحوادث ، تنبنى على اختلاف النسب ، المتولدة من اختلاف جهات الحركة ، فلتكن الحركة الأولى مغربية ، وما عداها مشرقية ، وقد حصل به الاختلاف ، ويحصل به تفاوت النسب ، فلم تعينت جهة واحدة ؟ ! وهذه الاختلافات لا تستدعى إلا أصل الاختلاف فأما جهة بعينها ، فليست بأولى من نقيضها ، فى هذا الغرض .
And some of them said: Since its perfection is obtained by motion, from whichever direction, and the order of terrestrial events requires difference of motions and determination of directions, the motive for it to the origin of motion was drawing near to God Almighty, and the motive to the direction of motion was the outpouring of good upon the lower world. And this is false, in two respects: First, that if this can be imagined, then let it be judged that the requirement of its nature is rest, avoiding motion and change, and this is truly an imitation of God Almighty, for He is transcendent above change, and motion is change. Rather, He chose motion for the outpouring of good, for others would benefit by it, and motion is not burdensome to Him, nor does it tire Him. So what prevents this imagination? Second, that events are built upon the difference of relations generated from the difference of directions of motion. So let the first motion be western, and the others eastern, and difference is thereby obtained, and variation of relations is obtained. So why was one direction specifically determined? These differences require only the fact of difference, not a particular direction, which is not more appropriate than its opposite for this purpose.
Source pages p. 224

Commentary
This passage presents a rejoinder from some philosophers and Ghazali's two-fold refutation. The philosophers argue that celestial bodies move to perfect themselves (for proximity to God) and that the specific direction of motion is to benefit the sublunary world through ordered events. Ghazali counters with two points. First, if one can imagine that the celestial bodies move for the benefit of others, one could just as well imagine that their nature requires rest to avoid change, which would also be an imitation of God's transcendence. Since God chose motion for the benefit of others without being affected, it is equally conceivable that the celestial bodies do so; thus the argument is arbitrary. Second, Ghazali argues that what matters for generating events is difference of directions, not the specific direction. If the first motion were western and the others eastern, the same difference would exist. Therefore, there is no reason to assign a particular direction; any direction would serve the alleged purpose. Hence, the philosophers' specific claim is groundless.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The philosophers' explanation for the specific direction of motion is necessary and grounded in reason.
Correction: Ghazali shows it is arbitrary: any direction would suffice, and an alternative (rest) is equally conceivable.

Checkpoint
What is the second refutation Ghazali gives against the philosophers' rejoinder?
A) The celestial bodies are inanimate and cannot benefit others.
B) The difference of directions is the only requirement, not the specific direction.
C) Motion is impossible for celestial bodies because they are eternal.
D) The lower world does not need any benefit from celestial motions.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali demonstrates that the philosophers' claim about celestial motion direction is arbitrary and unfounded, using rational parity and logical necessity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever justified a choice or action as 'necessary' when it was actually one of many equally valid options? What would it take for you to admit that your preference is not the only rational one?
?
Ghazali challenges the philosophers' assumption that motion is perfection and rest is deficiency. In your own life, what do you assume to be 'perfection' that might actually be an imitation of God's transcendence? How can you resist the pressure to be constantly active?
?
The philosophers claimed a noble purpose (ordering the world) for a specific direction, but Ghazali shows the direction is arbitrary. Where in your life do you attach a noble cause to a particular method, while ignoring that other methods could achieve the same good? Are you willing to let go of your attachment to that specific way?

The Necessity of Particular Wills for Celestial Motions
Ghazali presents the philosophers' argument that celestial motions require particular wills.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the philosophers argue that circular motion requires a particular will, and why a universal will cannot produce particular motions.

Core Idea
Universal wills cannot produce particular effects; particular motions need particular wills.

The philosophers claim that the circular motion of the celestial sphere is voluntary. Since every actual existent is a particular, a universal will cannot directly bring it about. Therefore, the celestial sphere must have a particular will for each motion. This lesson unpacks that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice the structure: premise, argument, conclusion.
The passage begins with a reference to a previous objection, but focus on the reasoning starting from 'it is established that circular motion is voluntary'.
2
Identify the key premises
Find the statements: (1) circular motion is voluntary, (2) will follows the object of will, (3) universal will cannot produce a particular existent.
3
Follow the inference to particular will
Understand why a universal will cannot produce a particular motion, leading to the necessity of a particular will for each motion.
The universal will has a uniform relation to all particulars, so no specific particular can issue from it.
4
Note the role of corporeal power
Observe that the particular will requires a conception (knowledge) of the particular motion, and such knowledge must be by a corporeal power because particulars are only perceived that way.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
استحالته ، فنطالبهم بالدليل عليه ، فإنه تحكم في نفسه استدلوا فيه ، بأن قالوا : ثبت أن الحركة الدورية إرادية ، والإرادة تتبع المراد ، والمراد الكلى ، لا تتوجه إليه إلا إرادة كلية ، والإرادة الكلية ، لا يصدر منها شيء ، فإن كل موجود بالفعل ، معين جزئى ، والإرادة الكلية ، نسبتها إلى آحاد الجزئيات ، على وتيرة واحدة ، فلا يصدر عنها شيء جزئى ، بل لابد من إرادة جزئية للحركة المعينة فللفلك فى كل حركة جزئية معينة ، من نقطة إلى نقطة معينة ، إرادة جزئية لتلك الحركة ، فله لا محالة تصور لتلك الحركات الجزئية ، بقوة جسمانية ، إذ الجزئيات لا تدرك إلا بالقوى الجسمانية ، فإن كل إرادة ، فمن ضرورتها تصور لذلك المراد ، أي علم به ، سواء كان جزئيا أو كليا . ومهما كان للفلك ، تصور الجزئيات الحركات وإحاطة بها ، أحاط لا محالة بما يلزم منها ، من اختلاف النسب مع الأرض ، من كون بعض أجزائه طالعة ، وبعضها غاربة ، وبعضها في وسط السماء ، فوق قوم ، وتحت قدم قوم ، وكذلك يعلم ما يلزم من اختلاف النسب ، التي تتجدد بالحركة ، من التثليث والتسديس ، والمقابلة والمقارنة ، إلى غير ذلك من الحوادث السماوية . وسائر الحوادث الأرضية ، تستند إلى الحوادث السماوية ، إما بغير واسطة ، وإما بواسطة واحدة ، وإما بوسائط كثيرة . وعلى الجملة ، فكل حادث ، فله سبب حادث ، إلى أن ينقطع التسلسل ، بالارتقاء إلى الحركة السماوية الأبدية ، التي بعضها سبب للبعض فإذن الأسباب والمسببات ، في سلسلتها ، تنتهى إلى الحركات الجزئية الدورية السماوية ، فالمتصور للحركات ، متصور للوازمها متصور للوازمها ، ولوازم لوازمها إلى آخر السلسلة . فبهذا يطلع على ما يحدث
Its impossibility, so we demand proof from them, for it is arbitrary in itself. They argued regarding it, saying: It is established that circular motion is voluntary, and will follows the object of will, and the universal object of will is only directed to a universal will, and a universal will does not produce anything, for every actual existent is a particular individual, and the universal will has a uniform relation to individual particulars, so nothing particular issues from it; rather, a particular will is necessary for the particular motion. So for the celestial sphere in every particular motion, from a point to a particular point, there is a particular will for that motion. Therefore, it necessarily has a conception of those particular motions by a corporeal power, since particulars are only perceived by corporeal powers. For every will necessarily entails a conception of that object of will, i.e., knowledge of it, whether particular or universal. And whenever the celestial sphere has conception of the particular motions and encompasses them, it necessarily encompasses what follows from them of the varying relations with the earth, such as some parts being rising, some setting, some in the middle of the sky above some people and under the feet of others, and likewise it knows what follows from the varying relations that are renewed by motion, such as trine, sextile, opposition, conjunction, and other celestial events. And all terrestrial events are based on celestial events, either without intermediary, or through one intermediary, or through many intermediaries. In summary, every event has a preceding event as its cause, until the chain ends by ascending to the eternal celestial motion, some of which is cause for some. Therefore, causes and effects in their chain terminate at the particular circular celestial motions. So the one who conceives the motions conceives their necessary consequences and the consequences of those consequences to the end of the chain. By this he comes to know what occurs.
Source pages p. 227

Commentary
This passage is a proof presented by the philosophers (the falasifa) to defend their claim that the celestial sphere has knowledge of particulars. Ghazali is stating their argument, which he will later critique. The argument begins by asserting that circular motion is voluntary (إرادية). Will must follow the object of will (المراد). If the object of will is universal, it would require a universal will, but a universal will cannot produce a particular existent, because every actual existent is a particular individual. The universal will has a uniform relation to all particulars, so no single particular can issue from it. Therefore, for each particular motion of the celestial sphere, there must be a particular will. This particular will necessarily entails a conception (تصور) of that particular motion, which must be by a corporeal power (قوة جسمانية) because particulars are only perceived by corporeal powers. Once the celestial sphere has conception and encompassing knowledge of these particular motions, it necessarily knows all the consequences that follow from them, such as the varying positions relative to the earth (rising, setting, etc.) and the resulting celestial configurations (trine, sextile, etc.). Moreover, all terrestrial events are caused by celestial events, either directly or through intermediaries. The chain of causes and effects ultimately leads back to the eternal circular motions. Therefore, the being that conceives these motions conceives all their consequences to the end of the chain, and thus knows everything that occurs.

Key Terms
إرادة كلية
إرادة جزئية
المراد الكلى
القوى الجسمانية
التصور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that a universal will can directly will a particular event.
Correction: The philosophers argue that a universal will cannot produce a particular because it relates equally to all particulars, so no specific particular is determined.

Checkpoint
Why do the philosophers argue that the celestial sphere must have a particular will for each motion?
A) Because a universal will can only produce universal effects, not particular motions.
B) Because the celestial sphere is corporeal and thus requires many wills.
C) Because the celestial sphere is ignorant of particulars.
D) Because all wills are universal by nature.

Key Takeaway
The celestial sphere requires a particular will for each motion because a universal will cannot produce a particular effect.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The philosophers argue that a universal will cannot produce a particular motion. Examine your own daily actions: do you operate with vague general intentions, or do you cultivate specific, conscious intentions for each act of worship and daily task? How might the lack of particular intention diminish the quality of your deeds? Commit today to forming a specific intention (niyyah) before every action, even small ones.
?
The celestial sphere is said to have a conception of each particular motion by a corporeal power. Consider your own awareness: do you truly 'conceive' of the particulars of your life—the details of your prayer, your work, your relationships—or do you often drift through them absentmindedly? What would change if you approached each moment with the same particular attention that the philosophers attribute to the heavens? Resolve to be fully present in your next act of worship.
?
If the celestial bodies require a particular will for each motion, what does that say about the One who set them in motion? Does your understanding of God's will accommodate His direct involvement in every particular event, or do you tend to think of Him as a distant cause? Let this argument humble you to see God's hand in the smallest details of your life.
