Celestial Knowledge of All Events Through Causal Chains
Ghazali presents the philosophers' argument that celestial knowledge of motions yields knowledge of all events.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the philosophers argue that the celestial sphere's knowledge of particular motions leads to knowledge of all celestial and terrestrial events.

Core Idea
Knowing the motions means knowing all consequences, down to every event on earth.

Having established that the celestial sphere has a particular will and conception of its motions, the philosophers now argue that this conception necessarily includes knowledge of all consequences of those motions. Since all terrestrial events are caused by celestial motions, the sphere knows everything that occurs. This lesson traces that causal chain.

Guided Path
1
Read the second part of the passage
Read the Arabic passage from 'ومهما كان للفلك' to the end, along with its translation.
This part builds on the first: if the sphere knows motions, it knows their effects.
2
Identify the types of consequences
The passage first lists consequences relating to the earth (rising, setting, positions) and then celestial configurations (trine, sextile, etc.).
3
Trace the causal chain to terrestrial events
Note the claim that all terrestrial events are based on celestial events, directly or through intermediaries, forming a chain ending in the eternal circular motions.
The chain ends in the circular motions because they are the ultimate cause.
4
Understand the conclusion
If the sphere conceives all motions, it conceives all consequences to the end of the chain, thus knowing everything that occurs.

Causality and human ignorance of future events
Ghazali explains the causal principle and human ignorance of future events.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' deterministic causal principle and why they believe celestial souls know future events while humans do not.

Core Idea
Knowledge of all causes would yield knowledge of all effects, but humans cannot grasp all celestial causes.

Imam Ghazali presents the philosophers' view on causality and future knowledge. According to them, every event is necessitated by its cause; ignorance of future stems from ignorance of causes. The passage explains how celestial souls, through knowledge of the First Cause, possess comprehensive knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the literal translation. Pay attention to the logical flow of the argument.
Notice the deterministic language: 'its occurrence is necessitated by its cause.'
2
Identify the causal principle
Find the statement that every event is necessitated by its cause. Understand that this is the foundation for the philosophers' claim about knowledge.
3
Analyze the examples
The passage gives three examples: fire meeting cotton, eating leading to satiety, and stepping on a treasure. Consider how each illustrates determinism.
These examples show that if we knew the causes, we would know the effects.
4
Reflect on celestial souls
The final part explains why celestial souls know future events: they know the First Cause and all its consequences. Contrast this with human limitation.
This sets up Ghazali's later critique of the philosophers.

فإن كل ما يحدث ، فحدوثه واجب عن علته ، مهما تحققت العلة جميع ونحن إنما لا نعلم ما يقع في المستقبل ، لأنا لا نعلم جميع أسبابها ، ولو علمنا المسببات ، فإنا مهما علمنا ، أن النار ستلتقى الأسباب ، لعلمنا جميع بالقطن مثلا ، في وقت معين ، نعلم احتراق القطن ، ومهما علمنا ، أن شخصاً سيأكل نعلم أنه سيشبع ، وإذا علمنا أن شخصاً ، سيتخطى الموضع الفلاني ، الذي فيه كنز مغطى بشيء خفيف ، إذا مشى عليه الماشي ، تعتر رجله في الكنز ، ويعرفه ، نعلم أنه سيستغنى أنه سيستغنى بوجود الكنز المسبب ولكن هذه الأسباب لا نعلمها ، وربما نعلم بعضها ، فيقع لنا حدس بوقوع ، فإن عرفنا أغلبها وأكثرها ، حصل لنا ظن ظاهر بالوقوع ، فلو حصل لنا العلم بجميع الأسباب ، لحصلت المعرفة بجميع المسببات ، إلا أن السماويات كثيرة ، ثم لها اختلاط بالحوادث الأرضية ، وليس في القوة البشرية الاطلاع عليها ؛ ونفوس السموات مطلعة عليها ، لاطلاعها على السبب الأول ، ولوازمها ولوازم لوازمها ، إلى آخر السلسلة
For everything that occurs, its occurrence is necessitated by its cause, whenever the cause is fully realized. All [causes] – and we only do not know what will occur in the future because we do not know all its causes. If we knew the [future] effects, then whenever we know that fire will meet [combustible material] – for example, cotton at a specific time – we know the burning of the cotton. And whenever we know that a person will eat, we know that he will be satisfied. And if we know that a person will step on a certain spot where there is a treasure covered by something light, and when the walker walks on it, his foot stumbles on the treasure and he knows it, we know that he will become rich by the existence of that treasure, the effect. But we do not know these causes; perhaps we know some of them, and so we have a conjecture of its occurrence. If we know most of them, we obtain a strong opinion of occurrence. So if we obtained knowledge of all causes, we would obtain knowledge of all effects. However, celestial [causes] are many, and they are mixed with terrestrial events, and it is not within human power to investigate them. But the souls of the celestial spheres are informed of them, due to their knowledge of the First Cause and its necessary consequences and the consequences of those consequences to the end of the chain.
Source pages p. 227

Commentary
This passage is an explanation of the causal principle underlying the philosophers' claim about celestial knowledge. Ghazali presents their deterministic worldview: every event is necessitated by its cause, and if the cause is fully realized, the effect is inevitable. The reason humans do not know future events is that they do not know all the causes. To illustrate, the philosophers give examples: if one knows that fire will meet cotton, one knows burning; if one knows that a person will eat, one knows satiety; if one knows that a person will step on a hidden treasure, one knows he will become rich. Thus, if we knew all causes, we would know all effects. However, the celestial causes are vast and intertwined with terrestrial events, and human power cannot grasp them. But the celestial souls (نفوس السموات) are informed of all these causes because they know the First Cause (السبب الأول) and all its necessary consequences (لوازمه) down the chain. This explains how the celestial sphere can have comprehensive knowledge.

المسبب
السبب الأول
نفوس السموات

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the philosophers deny human free will entirely; here they emphasize determinism only from the perspective of knowledge of causes, not necessarily denying free will.
Correction: The passage focuses on epistemic determinism: if we knew all causes, we would know all effects. It does not explicitly address metaphysical freedom or determinism of action.

Checkpoint
According to the philosophers, why do we not know future events?
A) Because future events are not caused.
B) Because we do not know all the causes.
C) Because celestial causes are simple.
D) Because God hides them intentionally.

Key Takeaway
According to the philosophers, comprehensive knowledge of all causes would allow knowledge of all future events, but humans lack access to the full chain of causes, while celestial souls have it through the First Cause.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You live your life planning and worrying about the future as if you control all its causes. The philosophers say that only those who know the entire causal chain – which only the celestial souls do – can have certainty. Where does your trust in your own plans really lie? How can you shift your reliance from your limited knowledge to the One who knows all causes?
?
The passage shows that even the best human knowledge only gives strong opinion, not certainty. When you are anxious about tomorrow, are you seeking certainty from your own analysis or from Allah? What would change if you treated every unknown future as an opportunity to trust His knowledge?
?
You claim to believe Allah is the First Cause, but do you live as if His decree is more certain than the causes you can see? Identify one specific worry you have about the future and ask yourself: is it because of a lack of causes I can control, or a lack of trust in the Cause of all causes? What concrete step can you take today to act on that trust?

Why Humans Cannot Know Future Events: The Philosophers' Causal Argument
The philosophers explain that ignorance of causes prevents knowledge of future events.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' causal explanation for human ignorance of future events and their view that celestial souls possess such knowledge.

Core Idea
Future knowledge requires complete causal knowledge, which humans lack but celestial souls have.

This passage presents the philosophers' account of why humans are ignorant of future events. They argue that knowledge of the future depends on knowing all causes in a chain. Through examples like fire burning cotton or eating leading to fullness, they show that if we know the cause, we know the effect. However, humans only know a few causes, leading to conjecture or opinion, while celestial souls have complete causal knowledge from the First Cause.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Pay attention to the examples and the logical flow.
Some words are unclear due to OCR; focus on the overall argument.
2
Identify the causal principle and examples
Notice that the philosophers state: if we know the cause, we know the effect. They give three examples: fire and cotton, eating and satiety, and walking over a hidden treasure. Identify how each illustrates the principle.
3
Understand the distinction between human and celestial knowledge
The passage contrasts limited human knowledge (some causes → conjecture, most causes → opinion) with complete celestial knowledge. Celestial souls know because they know the First Cause and its entire chain of consequences.
Reflect on why humans cannot attain such knowledge according to this view.
4
Reflect on the common misunderstanding
Review the misconception: the philosophers do not deny all future knowledge, but only claim that humans lack the causal information needed for certainty. They allow for conjecture and opinion.

ونحن إنما لا نعلم ما يقع في المستقبل، لأنا لا نعلم جميع أسبابها، ولو علمنا المسببات، فإنا مهما علمنا أن النار ستلتقي الأسباب، لعلمنا جميع [؟] بالقطن مثلا في وقت معين، نعلم احتراق القطن، ومهما علمنا أن شخصاً سيأكل نعلم أنه سيشبع، وإذا علمنا أن شخصاً سيتخطى الموضع الفلاني الذي فيه كنز مغطى بشيء خفيف، إذا مشى عليه الماشي [؟] رجله في الكنز ويعرفه، نعلم أنه سيستغنى بوجود الكنز المسبب. ولكن هذه الأسباب لا نعلمها، وربما نعلم بعضها، فيقع لنا حدس بوقوع، فإن عرفنا أغلبها وأكثرها، حصل لنا ظن ظاهر بالوقوع، فلو حصل لنا العلم بجميع الأسباب، لحصلت المعرفة بجميع المسببات، إلا أن السماويات كثيرة، ثم لها اختلاط بالحوادث الأرضية، وليس في القوة البشرية الاطلاع عليها؛ ونفوس السموات مطلعة عليها، لاطلاعها على السبب الأول، ولوازمها ولوازم لوازمها، إلى آخر السلسلة.
We only do not know what will occur in the future because we do not know all its causes. If we knew the effects, then whenever we know that fire will meet the causes, we would know all [؟] with cotton, for example, at a specific time, we know the burning of cotton. And whenever we know that a person will eat, we know that he will become full. And if we know that a person will cross a certain place in which there is a treasure covered by a light object, when a walker walks on it, his foot [؟] in the treasure and he knows it, we know that he will become rich by the existence of the caused treasure. But these causes we do not know. Sometimes we know some of them, so we have a conjecture of occurrence. If we know most of them, we obtain a predominant opinion of occurrence. So if we obtained knowledge of all causes, we would obtain knowledge of all effects, except that the heavenly [causes] are many, and they have intermixing with earthly events, and it is not within human power to be informed of them. The celestial souls are informed of them because of their knowledge of the First Cause and its necessary consequences and the necessary consequences of those consequences, to the end of the chain.

Commentary
This passage is an explanation by the philosophers of why humans are ignorant of the future. They argue that knowledge of future events depends on knowledge of all causal chains. They give examples of mundane causal knowledge: fire and cotton, eating and satiety, walking over a hidden treasure. If we know the cause, we know the effect. But in reality, we do not know all causes. Sometimes we know some, leading to conjecture or opinion. If we knew all causes, we would know all effects. However, the heavenly causes are numerous and intertwined with earthly events, beyond human capacity. Only the celestial souls (nufus al-samawat) are aware of them because they are connected to the First Cause (al-sabab al-awwal) and its entire chain of consequences. This sets up the basis for their later argument about prophetic knowledge.

Universal Will Suffices for Celestial Motion
Ghazali argues that celestial motion needs only universal will via analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's argument that celestial motion requires only a universal will, not particular wills, and how he uses analogies to support this.

Core Idea
Celestial motion is uniform, so a universal will suffices.

Ghazali continues his critique of the philosophers by addressing their claim that celestial motion requires a particular will for each moment. He concedes that for human actions, particular wills are needed, but argues that the heavens are different: a single body with a single direction. Through analogies with a stone's fall, he shows that a universal will is sufficient. This lesson unpacks that argument.

Guided Path
1
Understand the philosophers' claim
Ghazali presents the philosophers' premise that celestial motion requires particular wills. Recognize this as the view he is refuting.
2
Explore the Hajj analogy
Ghazali uses the example of a person going to Hajj to explain how particular wills work for human actions. Note the role of changing directions and conceptions.
3
Apply the analogy to celestial motion
Compare with celestial motion where direction is single and body is one. Understand why a universal will suffices, like the stone falling downward.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقدمة الثانية ، أنه إن سلم ذلك ، مسامحة لكم به ، فقولكم إنه يفتقر إلى جزئى للحركات الجزئية ، فغير مسلم ، إذ ليس ثم جزء عندكم الجسم ، فإنه شيء واحد ، وإنما يتجزأ بالوهم ؛ ولا في الحركة ، فإنها واحدة بالاتصال ، فيكفي تشوقها إلى استيفاء [؟] الممكنة لها ، كما ذكروه ويكفيها التصور الكلى ، والإرادة الكلية . ولنمثل للإرادة الكلية والجزئية ، مثالا ، لتفهيم غرضهم. فإذا كان للإنسان غرض كلى ، فى أن يحج بيت الله تعالى مثلاً ، فهذه الإرادة الكلية لا تصدر منها الحركة ، لأن الحركة تقع جزئية ، في جهة مخصوصة بمقدار مخصوص ، بل لابد فى الحركة الإرادية، من إرادة جزئية ، ولا يزال يتجدد للإنسان ، فى توجهه إلى البيت ، تصور بعد تصور ، للمكان الذي يتخطاه ، والجهة التي يسلكها ، ويتبع كل تصور جزئى ، إرادة جزئية ، للحركة عن المحل الموصول إليه بالحركة . فهذا ما أرادوه بالإرادة الجزئية ، التابعة للتصور الجزئى ، وهو مسلم لهم في الحج ، لأن الجهات متعددة ، فى التوجه إلى مكة ، والمسافة غير متعينة ، فيفتقر تعين مكان عن مكان ، وجهة عن جهة ، إلى إرادة أخرى جزئية . وأما الحركة السماوية ، فلها جهة واحدة ، فإن الكرة إنما تتحرك على نفسها ، وفى حيزها لا تجاوزه ، والحركة مرادة ، وليس ثمة إلا جهة واحدة ، وجسم واحد ، وصوب واحد ، فهو كهوى الحجر إلى أسفل ، فإنه يطلب الأرض في أقرب طريق ، وأقرب الطرق ، الخط المستقيم ، الذي هو عمود على الأرض فتعين الخط المستقيم فلم يفتقر فيه ، إلى تجدد سبب حادث ، سوى الطبيعة الكلية ، الطالبة للمركز ، مع تجدد القرب ، والبعد ، والوصول إلى حد ، والصدور عنه ، فكذلك يكفى فى تلك الحركة ، الإرادة الكلية للحركة ، ولا تفتقر إلى مزيد .
The second premise: that if that is granted, as a concession to you, then your statement that it needs particular [will] for particular motions is not granted, because there is no part in the body according to you – for it is one thing, and is only divided in estimation; nor in motion, for it is one by continuity. So its longing to fulfill the [؟] possible for it suffices, as they mentioned, and the universal conception and universal will suffice for it. Let us give an example of universal and particular will to clarify their intent. If a person has a universal purpose, such as to perform Hajj to the House of God, then this universal will does not produce the motion, because motion occurs as particular, in a specific direction by a specific amount. Rather, in voluntary motion, a particular will is necessary, and the person continually renews, in his heading toward the House, conception after conception of the place he traverses and the direction he takes, and each particular conception is followed by a particular will for movement from the place reached by motion. This is what they meant by particular will following particular conception, and it is granted to them in Hajj, because the directions are multiple in heading to Mecca, and the distance is not determined, so determining one place over another and one direction over another requires another particular will. As for celestial motion, it has one direction – for the sphere moves upon itself and within its own space, not exceeding it – and motion is willed, and there is only one direction, one body, and one side. So it is like the falling of a stone downward: it seeks the earth by the shortest path, and the shortest path is the straight line perpendicular to the earth. So the straight line is determined, and it does not require a renewed contingent cause except the universal nature seeking the center, along with the renewal of nearness and distance, reaching a limit and departing from it. Similarly, in that motion, the universal will for motion suffices, and it does not need anything more.
Source pages p. 230

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the philosophers' second premise, which claims that celestial motion requires particular wills for each particular motion. Ghazali argues that the celestial sphere is a single body with a single direction, so a universal will (al-irada al-kulliyya) suffices. He first explains what particular will is, using the example of a person performing Hajj: the universal purpose to go to Mecca does not produce the specific movements; rather, for each step, a particular conception of the next place and a particular will are needed, because the directions and distances vary. This he concedes for human voluntary actions. However, celestial motion is different: the sphere rotates on itself within its own space, with only one direction. He compares it to a stone falling straight down: the universal nature (tabi'a kulliyya) seeking the center suffices, without needing new causes for each moment. Similarly, a universal will for motion is sufficient for the heavens, because the direction is single and the motion is continuous. Therefore, the philosophers' claim that particular wills are necessary is not valid for celestial motion.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ghazali denies any will in celestial motion.
Correction: Ghazali affirms a universal will (al-irada al-kulliyya) for celestial motion, rejecting only the need for particular wills.

Why does Ghazali argue that celestial motion does not require particular wills?
A) Because celestial motion is involuntary.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Consider an area of your life where you are consistent and steady—like a regular devotion or habit. Do you see that as powered by a single, unified intention? Or do you treat each instance as a new battle? What would shift if you saw it as one continuous movement under a universal will?
?
If celestial motion requires only a universal will, what does that say about how you approach your own long-term goals? Are you creating particular wills for every small hurdle, exhausting yourself, or are you anchoring your actions in a single, strong purpose? Which approach is more reflective of tawheed in action?

Universal vs Particular Will in Celestial Motion
Ghazali refutes the need for particular will in celestial motion using example and analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why celestial motion requires only a universal will, not a series of particular wills, according to Ghazali's refutation of the philosophers.

Core Idea
Celestial motion is like a falling stone – one direction, one will.

Ghazali challenges the philosophers' claim that celestial motion needs a new particular will at each moment. He argues that since the celestial sphere moves continuously in one direction, a universal will suffices, just as a stone's fall to the center requires only its natural inclination. This lesson unpacks his reasoning through a detailed example and analogy.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Let Ghazali's own argument unfold before interpreting.
Notice the concessive tone at the start ('if that is granted').
2
Understand the counter-argument about continuity
Ghazali argues that since body and motion are continuous (according to the philosophers), there are no parts to require particular wills. A universal conception and will suffice.
This is a key move: accepting their premises but denying the conclusion.
3
Examine the Hajj example
Ghazali illustrates how earthly travel (like Hajj) requires particular wills because of varying directions and distances. Each leg of the journey demands a new particular intention.
He concedes the need for particular wills in such cases.
4
Contrast with celestial motion and the stone analogy
Celestial motion has only one direction (rotation on axis), like a stone falling straight down. No new causes are needed beyond the universal inclination.
The analogy reinforces that uniformity eliminates the need for particular wills.
5
Grasp the conclusion
Thus, a universal will is sufficient for celestial motion. The philosophers' objection is refuted.

Consider in the second premise: if that is granted, as a concession to you, then your statement that it needs a particular for the particular motions is not granted, because according to you there is no part in the body, for it is one thing, and it is only divided by imagination; nor in motion, for it is one through continuity, so its desire to complete the [؟] possible for it suffices, as they mentioned, and the universal conception and universal will suffice. And let us give an example of universal and particular will to clarify their purpose. If a person has a universal purpose, such as to perform Hajj to the House of Allah, then this universal will does not produce motion, because motion occurs as particular, in a specific direction and to a specific extent. Rather, voluntary motion necessarily requires a particular will, and the person continuously, in his journey to the House, acquires conception after conception of the place he traverses and the direction he takes, and each particular conception is followed by a particular will for motion from the place he has reached. This is what they meant by particular will, which follows particular conception, and it is conceded to them in Hajj, because the directions are multiple in the journey to Mecca, and the distance is not determined, so the specification of one place over another and one direction over another requires another particular will. As for celestial motion, it has only one direction, for the sphere moves upon itself and within its own space, not exceeding it. The motion is willed, and there is no direction but one, one body, and one axis. It is like the fall of a stone downward, for it seeks the earth by the shortest path, and the shortest path is the straight line that is perpendicular to the earth. So the straight line is determined, and it does not need a new cause to arise besides the universal nature seeking the center, along with the renewal of closeness, distance, reaching a limit, and departure from it. Thus, in that motion, the universal will for motion suffices, and it does not need more.
Source pages p. 230

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the philosophers' claim that celestial motion requires a particular will for each moment. Ghazali begins by addressing the second premise: even if we grant your argument about division, your claim that motion needs a particular for each part is not granted. According to you, the body and motion are continuous, so a universal conception and will suffice. He then gives an example: a person going to Hajj has a universal will, but this does not produce actual motion because motion is particular in direction and magnitude. Instead, particular volitions arise from particular conceptions of the next location. This is conceded for earthly travel because directions vary. However, celestial motion has only one direction – the sphere rotates on its axis without leaving its space. It is analogous to a stone falling straight down, which needs no additional causes beyond its natural tendency toward the center. Thus, a universal will is sufficient, and no particular will is needed.

Key Terms
إرادة كلية
إرادة جزئية
تصور كلي
تصور جزئي
حركة سماوية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All voluntary motion requires a series of particular wills for each moment.
Correction: Ghazali shows that celestial motion, having a single direction, only needs a universal will, analogous to a stone's fall.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, why does celestial motion not require a particular will for each moment?
A) Because the celestial sphere has no will
B) Because it has only one direction and is analogous to a falling stone
C) Because celestial motion is eternal and does not change
D) Because the philosophers' argument only applies to human motion

Key Takeaway
Celestial motion's uniformity allows a single universal will, unlike variable earthly motion which requires multiple particular wills.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are there areas in your life where you claim to have a universal will (like 'I want to be a good Muslim') but your actions are not particular? When have you allowed multiple directions to distract you from a single purpose, unlike celestial motion?
?
The stone's fall needs no additional will because its path is determined. Do you recognize that your own spiritual journey, if guided by a single sincere intention, could be just as straightforward? What unnecessary particular wills—distractions, hesitations, competing desires—do you introduce that complicate your path to Allah?
?
Ghazali argues that a universal will suffices for uniform motion. Reflect: Is your worship driven by a single, consistent will to please Allah, or is it fragmented by varying moods and circumstances? What would it take for your actions to become as focused as the celestial sphere's rotation?

Critique of the Third Premise: The Absurdity of Knowing All Consequences
Ghazali ridicules the philosophers' third premise with a vivid analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ghazali rejects the philosophers' claim that knowledge of particular movements entails knowledge of all their consequences, and how he uses a vivid analogy to expose its absurdity.

Core Idea
Knowing a movement does not require knowing every trivial detail it produces.

Ghazali now takes aim at the third premise of the philosophers — the idea that if the celestial soul knows the particular movements of the heavens, it must also know every consequence of those movements. He considers this an extremely far-fetched claim and illustrates its absurdity with a relatable example from everyday human movement. This passage shows his method of reductio ad absurdum.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow of Ghazali's argument. Then read the English translation to ensure understanding.
Notice the strong language Ghazali uses: 'pure nonsense', 'clear nonsense'.
2
Identify the philosophers' premise
Identify the statement that Ghazali is critiquing: 'If he conceives of particular movements, he also conceives of their consequences and necessary concomitants.'
This premise is the target of the entire passage.
3
Examine Ghazali's analogy
Look at the example of a person walking and the list of details the premise would require knowing. How does this illustrate the absurdity?
Consider the infinite regress of trivial knowledge implied.
4
Understand why it's 'clear nonsense'
Reflect on why Ghazali says no sane person would imagine this. Think about the practical impossibility of knowing all those details simultaneously.
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum argument.
5
Connect to the larger argument
Consider how this critique undermines the philosophers' case for divine knowledge. If this premise is absurd, their entire argument collapses.
Ghazali is showing that the philosophers' conception of divine knowledge is incoherent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقدمة الثالثة : وهى التحكم البعيد جداً ، قولهم : إنه إذا تصور الحركات الجزئية ، تصوّر أيضاً ، توابعها ولوازمها ، وهذا هوس محض ، كقول القائل : إن الإنسان إذا تحرك ، وعرف حركته ، ينبغى أن يعرف ، ما يلزم من حركته ، من موازاة ومجاوزة ، وهو نسبته إلى الأجسام ، التي فوقه وتحته ومن جوانبه ، وإنه إذا مشى في شمس ، ينبغى أن يعلم المواضع ، التي يقع عليها ظله ، والمواضع التي لا يقع عليها ، وما يحصل عن ظله ، من البرودة ، بقطع الشعاع في تلك المواضع ، وما يحصل من الانضغاط لأجزاء الأرض تحت قدمه ، وما يحصل من التفريق فيها ، وما يحصل فى أخلاطه فى الباطن ، من الاستحالة ، بسبب الحركة ، إلى الحرارة وما يستحيل من أجزائه إلى العرق ، وهلم جرا ، إلى جميع الحوادث في بدنه وفى غير بدنه ، مما الحركة علة فيه ، أو شرط ، أو مهئ ومعد ، وهو هوس بين، لا يتخيله عاقل ، ولا يغتر به إلا جاهل، وإلى هذا، يرجع هذا التحكم .
The third premise: It is the extremely far-fetched arbitrary claim, their saying: 'If he conceives of particular movements, he also conceives of their consequences and necessary concomitants.' And this is pure nonsense, like the saying of someone: 'If a man moves and knows his movement, he should know what follows from his movement: alignment and surpassing, and his relation to bodies above him, below him, and from his sides; and if he walks in the sun, he should know the places where his shadow falls and the places where it does not fall, and what results from his shadow of coolness by cutting off radiation in those places, and what results from the compression of parts of the earth under his foot, and what results from dispersion in it, and what results in his internal humors of change, due to movement, into heat, and what changes from his parts into sweat, and so on, to all events in his body and outside his body, for which movement is a cause, or condition, or preparer and predisposer.' And this is clear nonsense; no sane person imagines it, and none is deceived by it except an ignorant person. And to this the arbitrary claim reduces.
Source pages p. 231

Commentary
This passage is a critique of the philosophers' third premise in their argument about the celestial soul's knowledge. Ghazali states that this premise is an extremely far-fetched arbitrary claim. The premise asserts that if one conceives of particular movements, one also necessarily conceives of all their consequences and necessary concomitants. Ghazali declares this pure nonsense. He illustrates with a vivid analogy: if a person moves and knows his movement, the premise would imply he must know every detail resulting from that motion—such as his spatial relations to surrounding bodies, the exact places his shadow falls and does not fall when walking in the sun, the cooling effect of his shadow, the compression and displacement of earth under his feet, and even internal bodily changes like humors turning to heat and parts turning to sweat, and so on infinitely. Ghazali calls this clear nonsense that no rational person would imagine; only an ignorant person could be deceived by it. He concludes that the entire arbitrary claim reduces to this absurdity.

علة
شرط
مهئ ومعد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Knowing a cause naturally implies knowing all its effects.
Correction: Ghazali shows this leads to an infinite regress of trivial details that no finite mind can grasp.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, what is the consequence of the philosophers' third premise about knowing movements?
A) It is a well-reasoned argument.
B) It leads to an infinite list of trivial details that no sane person can know.
C) It proves the celestial soul's omniscience.
D) It is a necessary condition for prophecy.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali rejects the third premise because it leads to absurdly infinite knowledge requirements, revealing the philosophers' claim as arbitrary.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on your daily life, how often do you assume you know all the repercussions of your actions? Consider the analogy of walking in the sun: you never think about your shadow cooling the ground or the earth compressing under your feet. What does this reveal about your actual ignorance? How can you cultivate humility about the hidden consequences of even your simplest deeds?
?
Ghazali calls the philosophers' claim 'pure nonsense' and says no sane person is deceived by it except an ignorant one. Examine your own heart: do you sometimes secretly believe that you understand events fully because you know a small part of their cause? In what area of your life do you need to repent of intellectual arrogance and acknowledge that only Allah knows all?

Ghazali's Critique of Celestial Souls' Perfect Knowledge
Ghazali refutes the philosophers' argument for celestial souls' perfect knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's critique of the philosophers' claim that celestial souls have perfect knowledge of all particulars without distraction, and his argument that such certainty is unfounded.

Core Idea
Celestial souls may have their own distractions—we cannot claim certainty.

This passage is a classic example of Ghazali's dialectical method. He begins by posing a logical difficulty about infinite knowledge in a finite created intellect. He then anticipates a possible objection that this would apply to God's knowledge, but quickly sets that aside. The philosophers counter that human souls are distracted by bodily passions, while celestial souls are free from these. Ghazali's response is devastating: how do you know they have no distractions? Perhaps their worship of the First is itself an all-consuming distraction. More fundamentally, why should we assume that only our familiar distractions exist? There could be unknown impediments. This lesson walks through the argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its English translation to grasp the overall flow of the argument. Notice the objection-and-answer structure.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify Ghazali's initial problem
Ghazali points out that a created soul cannot simultaneously know infinite particulars without succession—this seems absurd. Note his quick deferral regarding God's knowledge.
This sets the stage for the argument.
3
Follow the philosophers' objection
The philosophers argue that human souls are distracted by bodily passions, but celestial souls are free from such distractions, so they can know all things. Understand their premise.
This is a plausible defense.
4
Examine Ghazali's counter-questions
Ghazali responds with a series of questions: How do you know celestial souls have no distraction? Could their worship of the First be a distraction? Why limit impediments to bodily ones?
These questions undermine certainty.
5
Reflect on the argument from possibility
Ghazali concludes that the philosophers' claim at best yields possibility, not certainty. Since possibility remains, their assertion of certainty is invalid.
This is the key methodological point.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
جميع الجزئيات في الاستقبال إلى غير نهاية؟! وكيف يجتمع نفس مخلوق في حال واحدة من غير تعاقب علوم جزئية مفصلة لا نهاية لأعدادها ولا غاية لآحادها؟ ومن لم يشهد له عقله باستحالة ذلك فلييأس عقله من... فإن قلبوا علينا هذا في علم الله تعالى فليس تعلق علم الله تعالى بمعلوماته بالاتفاق على نحو تعلق العلوم التي هي للمخلوقات. بل مهما دار نفس الفلك دورة نفس الإنسان كان من قبيل نفس الإنسان؛ فإنه شاركه في كونه مدركاً للجزئيات بواسطة، فإن لم يلتحق به قطعاً كان الغالب على الظن أنه من قبيله، فإن لم يكن غالباً على الظن فهو ممكن، والإمكان يبطل دعواهم القطع بما قطعوا به.

فإن قيل: حق النفس الإنسانية في جوهرها أن تدرك أيضاً الأشياء ولكن اشتغالها بنتائج الشهوة والغضب والحرص والحقد والحسد والجوع والألم وبالجملة عوارض البدن وما تورده الحواس عليه، حتى إذا أقبلت النفس الإنسانية على شيء واحد شغلها عن غيره. وأما النفوس الفلكية فبريئة عن هذه الصفات، لا يعتريها شاغل ولا يستغرقها هم وألم وإحساس، فعرفت جميع الأشياء.

قلنا: وبم عرفتم أنه لا شاغل لها؟ وهلا كانت عبادتها واشتياقها إلى الأول مستغرقاً لها وشاغلاً لها عن تصور الجزئيات المفصلة! أو ما الذي يحيل تقدير مانع آخر سوى الغضب والشهوة وهذه الموانع المحسوسة؟! ومن أين عرفتم انحصار المانع في القدر الذي شاهدناه أنفسنا؟! وفي العقلاء شواغل من علو الهمة وطلب الرئاسة ما يستحيل تصوره عند الأطفال ولا يعتقدونها شاغلاً ومانعاً، فمن أين يعرف استحالة ما يقوم مقامها في النفوس الفلكية.

هذا ما أردنا أن نذكره في العلوم الملقبة عندهم بالإلهية، وهي ست عشرة مسألة، والحمد لله وحده، وصلى الله على نبيه محمد وسلم.
All particulars in the future, without end?! And how can a created soul gather, in a single state, without succession, detailed particular sciences, no end to their numbers and no limit to their individuals? And whoever his intellect does not testify to the impossibility of that, let his intellect despair of... For if they turn this back on us regarding the knowledge of Allah the Exalted, the attachment of the knowledge of Allah the Exalted to His objects of knowledge is not, by agreement, like the attachment of the sciences that belong to creatures. Rather, whenever the soul of the sphere revolves like the revolution of the human soul, it belongs to the category of the human soul; for it shares with it in being a percipient of particulars through a medium. If it does not definitely join it, the predominant opinion is that it is of its category; if it is not predominant in opinion, then it is possible, and possibility invalidates their claim of certainty about what they have asserted. 

For it might be said: The essence of the human soul, in its substance, is also capable of apprehending things, but its preoccupation with the consequences of lust, anger, greed, malice, envy, hunger, pain, and in short the accidents of the body and what the senses bring upon it—such that when the human soul attends to one thing it diverts it from others. As for the celestial souls, they are free from these attributes; no preoccupation befalls them, nor do worry, pain, or sensation engross them, so they have known all things. 

We said: And how do you know that there is no preoccupation for them? Could it not be that their worship and longing for the First engrosses them and distracts them from conceiving detailed particulars? Or what prevents positing another impediment besides anger, lust, and these sensible impediments? And how do you know that impediments are limited to what we have observed in ourselves? Among rational people there are preoccupations of high ambition and seeking leadership, the conception of which is impossible for children, and they do not believe them to be preoccupying impediments. So how can one know the impossibility of what would take their place in the celestial souls? 

This is what we intended to mention regarding the sciences called by them the Divine Sciences, and they are sixteen issues. And praise be to Allah alone, and blessings be upon His Prophet Muhammad and peace.
Source pages p. 232

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer argument. Ghazali begins by stating a difficulty: how can a created soul simultaneously know an infinite number of detailed particulars without succession? He then anticipates a possible objection that this argument might also apply to God's knowledge, but he quickly defers that by noting that divine knowledge is not comparable to creaturely knowledge by consensus. He then turns to the philosophers' claim that the celestial souls (al-nufus al-falakiyya) are like human souls in perceiving particulars through a medium, but he argues that at best this yields probability, not certainty, and possibility suffices to undermine their dogmatic assertion. 

The philosophers then present an objection in the form of a 'qīla' (it might be said): the human soul in its essence can perceive things, but it is distracted by bodily appetites and sensations; celestial souls, being free from these, are not distracted and thus know all things. Ghazali's response (qulnā) is a series of counter-questions: how do you know they have no distraction? Could their worship and longing for the First (al-Awwal) itself be an all-engrossing distraction? Moreover, why limit impediments to bodily ones? There are other rational preoccupations, such as high ambition and desire for leadership, which children cannot even conceive as distractions. Thus, claiming certainty about the state of celestial souls is unjustified. 

The passage ends with Ghazali's concluding remark that this discussion concludes the section on the divine sciences (al-Ilāhiyyāt), which consist of sixteen issues.

Key Terms
النفوس الفلكية (celestial souls)
الجزئيات (particulars)
الشواغل (distractions/preoccupations)
الموانع (impediments)
الإمكان (possibility)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think Ghazali denies that celestial souls have any knowledge, but he only challenges the philosophers' claim of certainty.
Correction: Ghazali does not deny celestial souls' knowledge; he argues that their claim of certainty is unfounded because unknown impediments may exist.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali's response, what is a possible source of distraction for celestial souls that the philosophers overlooked?
A) Lust and anger
B) Worship and longing for the First
C) Sensation and pain
D) The need for sustenance

Key Takeaway
The philosophers' claim that celestial souls know all particulars without distraction is not certain because there could be unknown impediments, including their worship of the First.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You may pride yourself on your knowledge or intellect, but are you certain that you are not being distracted from the truth by your own desires, ambitions, or even your worship? Could your longing for Allah actually be a distraction from knowing Him as He truly is? Ask yourself: What 'preoccupations' in your life—whether bodily or spiritual—might be preventing you from seeing your own ignorance? Commit today to questioning your assumptions, just as Ghazali questioned the philosophers' certainty.
?
Ghazali points out that the philosophers assumed they knew about celestial souls based on their own limited experience. How often do you assume you know the state of others' hearts or the unseen realities because you have never experienced a certain kind of distraction? Could you be wrong? What one assumption about Allah or the unseen are you holding onto with false certainty? Write it down and test it against the Quran and Sunnah.
?
The philosophers claimed certainty without proof. But you too might claim certainty about matters of faith without examining your own potential distractions. Are you truly certain that your current level of worship, your current understanding of Tawhid, is free from hidden shirk or heedlessness? If not, what step will you take today to remove that distraction?

Ghazali's Four Disagreements with the Philosophers
Ghazali enumerates four disagreements with the philosophers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four specific issues on which Ghazali opposes the philosophers regarding causality, the soul, and bodily resurrection.

Core Idea
Ghazali opposes philosophers on four key issues: causality, soul's nature, its immortality, and bodily return.

In his Tahafut al-Falasifa, Ghazali clarifies that his disagreement with the philosophers is not over all their teachings but specifically over four issues. These issues touch on causality, the nature of the soul, its immortality, and the possibility of bodily resurrection. This passage presents the four points that will be the focus of his critique.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage carefully
Read the Arabic text once without analysis, then read the translation. Notice how Ghazali lists the four issues.
The list is introduced by 'في أربع مسائل'.
2
Analyze the first issue (causality)
Consider what it means to say cause and effect are necessarily linked. Ghazali rejects this necessity.
This is a key point in his critique of natural philosophy.
3
Analyze the second and third issues (soul)
The philosophers claim the soul is a self-subsistent essence that does not perish. Ghazali will challenge both claims.
These relate to the afterlife.
4
Understand the fourth issue (bodily return)
The philosophers deny the possibility of souls returning to bodies, which contradicts Islamic resurrection.
This is a major point of contention.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وليس يلزم مخالفتهم شرعاً في شيء من هذه العلوم ، وإنما نخالفهم من حملة هذه العلوم في أربع مسائل .
الأولى : حكمهم بأن هذا الاقتران المشاهد في الوجود بين الأسباب والمسببات اقتران تلازم بالضرورة ، فليس في المقدور ولا في الإمكان إيجاد السبب دون المسبب ولا وجود المسبب دون السبب .
الثانية : قولهم : إن النفوس الإنسانية جواهر قائمة بأنفسها ليست منطبعة في الجسم ، وإن معنى الموت انقطاع علاقتها عن البدن بانقطاع التدبير ، وإلا فهو قائم بنفسه في حال الموت ، وزعموا أن ذلك عرف بالبرهان العقلي .
الثالثة : قولهم : إن هذه النفوس يستحيل عليها العدم ، بل هي إذا وجدت فهي أبدية سرمدية لا يتصور فناؤها .
الرابعة : قولهم : يستحيل رد هذه النفوس إلى الأجساد .
And it is not necessary to oppose them legally in any of these sciences, but we oppose them, among the proponents of these sciences, in four issues. First: Their judgment that this observed conjunction in existence between causes and effects is a necessary conjunction of concomitance, so it is not within power or possibility to bring about the cause without the effect, nor the existence of the effect without the cause. Second: Their statement that human souls are self-subsistent essences not imprinted in the body, and that the meaning of death is the severance of their relationship with the body by the cessation of governance, but otherwise it is self-subsistent in the state of death, and they claimed that this is known by rational proof. Third: Their statement that these souls are impossible to perish, rather when they exist they are eternal, perpetual, their annihilation is inconceivable. Fourth: Their statement that it is impossible to return these souls to bodies.
Source pages p. 235

Commentary
This passage is an enumeration of four theological disagreements with the philosophers. Ghazali clarifies that legal opposition is not required regarding the previously mentioned sciences, but on four specific issues we oppose them. The first issue is their view of necessary causal connection: the relationship between cause and effect is one of necessary concomitance, making it impossible to separate them. The second is their doctrine of the soul: human souls are self-subsistent, not imprinted in the body, and death is merely the cessation of the soul's governance of the body. The third is the immortality of the soul: once existing, souls are eternal and cannot perish. The fourth is the denial of bodily resurrection: it is impossible for souls to return to bodies. These four topics are central to Ghazali's critique.

الأسباب والمسببات
اقتران تلازم
النفوس الإنسانية
جوهر
العدم
رد النفوس
البرهان العقلي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Ghazali rejects all forms of causality.
Correction: He actually rejects the necessary, inescapable connection, leaving room for miracles.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, how many issues does he disagree with the philosophers on?
A) three
B) four
C) five
D) seven

Key Takeaway
Ghazali identifies four key disagreements with the philosophers: causality, nature of the soul, immortality of the soul, and denial of bodily resurrection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that God is free to act independent of the natural laws you observe every day, or does your heart secretly think science binds His hands? This lesson forces you to choose: are you a determinist or do you trust in the Creator's absolute power?
?
When you think of your own soul, do you imagine it as something separate from your body that will simply float away after death? Ghazali opposes the philosophers' view of a self-subsistent soul. How does your belief about the soul affect your daily choices, especially regarding your body and its actions?
?
If bodily resurrection is impossible according to the philosophers, but you claim to believe in it, does your life reflect that belief? Do you treat your body as a temporary vessel or as something that will be resurrected and held to account? What one change can you make today to honour that belief?

Causality and the Limits of Miracles
Ghazali states the consequence of the first disagreement and lists the philosophers' accepted miracles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the philosophical stance on causality leads to denial of conventional miracles and the two exceptional types they accept.

Core Idea
Necessary causality denies miracles; philosophers save a place only for mental phenomena.

This passage from Ghazali's Tahafut shows the first disagreement's critical consequence: if causality is deemed necessary, miracles that break habits become impossible. Ghazali then reveals the philosophers' limited concession: they accept only two types of extraordinary phenomena rooted in human faculties, not divine interruption of nature.

Guided Path
1
Frame the stakes
Understand that the entire dispute about miracles hinges on whether causal connections are necessary or contingent.
If causality is necessary, no miracle can break it.
2
Examine the reinterpretations
See how philosophers reinterpret Quranic miracles: reviving the dead becomes removing ignorance, the staff becomes a rebuttal of doubts.
They deny physical miracles but allegorize them.
3
Identify the two accepted faculties
The philosophers admit two types of extraordinary phenomena: the imaginative faculty accessing the Preserved Tablet, and the intuitive power of swift reasoning.
These are psychological, not physical interruptions.
4
Reflect on the implications
Notice that prophethood is reduced to natural psychological traits, and no true miracle breaking nature is preserved.
This contrasts with the Islamic understanding of miracles as divine acts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإنما يلزم النزاع في الأولى من حيث إنه ينبني عليها إثبات المعجزات الخارقة للعادة ، من قلب العصا ثعباناً وإحياء الموتى وشق القمر . ومن جعل مجاري العادات لازمة لزوماً ضرورياً أحال جميع ذلك وأولوا ما في القرآن من إحياء الموتى وقالوا : أراد به إزالة موت الجهل بحياة العلم . وأولوا تلقف العصا سحر السحرة بإبطال الحجة الإلهية الظاهرة على يد موسى صلى الله عليه وسلم شبهات المنكرين . وأما شق القمر فربما أنكروا وجوده وزعموا أنه لم يتواتر .
ولم يثبت الفلاسفة من المعجزات الخارقة للعادة إلا ثلاثة أمور : أحدها : في القوة المتخيلة ، فإنهم زعموا أنها إذا استولت وقويت ولم تستغرقها الحواس بالاشتغال اطلعت على اللوح المحفوظ فانطبعت فيها صور الجزئيات الكائنة في المستقبل وذلك في اليقظة للأنبياء صلوات الله عليهم ولسائر الناس في النوم . فهذه خاصية النبوة التي هي للقوة المتخيلة .
الثانية : خاصية في القوة النظرية العقلية وهو راجع إلى قوة الحدس وهو سرعة الانتقال من معلوم إلى معلوم ، فرب ذكي إذ ذكر له المدلول تنبه للدليل .
And the dispute only arises concerning the first [issue] because upon it is based the establishment of miracles that break habitual patterns, such as turning the staff into a snake, reviving the dead, and splitting the moon. And whoever makes the courses of habit necessary with a necessary necessity deems all that impossible, and they reinterpret what is in the Quran concerning reviving the dead and said: He intended by it the removal of the death of ignorance through the life of knowledge. And they reinterpreted the swallowing up of the staff of the sorcerers' magic as nullifying the divine proof manifested at the hand of Moses, peace be upon him, against the doubts of the deniers. And as for the splitting of the moon, perhaps they denied its occurrence and claimed that it was not mutawatir. And the philosophers did not affirm from miracles that break habits except three matters: One of them: concerning the imaginative faculty, for they claimed that when it dominates and becomes strong and the senses do not preoccupy it, it becomes aware of the Preserved Tablet and the images of future particulars become imprinted in it, and that is in wakefulness for the prophets, blessings of God upon them, and for other people in sleep. So this is the property of prophethood belonging to the imaginative faculty. The second: a property in the theoretical rational faculty, which goes back to the power of intuition, which is swift transition from known to known. For sometimes a clever person, when the conclusion is mentioned to him, becomes aware of the proof.
Source pages p. 236

Commentary
This passage discusses the consequences of the first disagreement regarding causality. Ghazali explains that this issue is crucial because it underpins the acceptance of miracles that break habitual patterns: turning a staff into a snake, reviving the dead, and splitting the moon. Those who believe causal connections are absolutely necessary deny these miracles and reinterpret the Quran: they say reviving the dead means removing ignorance, and the staff swallowing the sorcerers' magic is nullifying doubts. They even deny the splitting of the moon, claiming it is not reliably transmitted. Then Ghazali states that the philosophers only accept three types of extraordinary miracles, although only two are listed here. The first is the imaginative faculty of prophets: when it is strong and not occupied by senses, it accesses the Preserved Tablet and perceives future particulars; this happens for prophets in wakefulness and for others in sleep. The second is a property of the theoretical rational faculty, related to intuition (hads): a swift transition from known to known. The third is not mentioned in this text.

القوة المتخيلة
اللوح المحفوظ

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Philosophers deny all miracles entirely.
Correction: They deny only miracles that break physical causality, but accept two types based on psychological faculties: imaginative access to the Preserved Tablet and rational intuition.

Philosophers reduce miracles to natural psychological faculties, denying any physical interruption of the natural order.

?

What is one area of your life where you rely on 'habit' more than on Allah's absolute power? Ask Him tonight to shatter that reliance.

The Philosophers' Four Doctrines and the Problem of Miracles
Ghazali enumerates four philosophers' doctrines and highlights the first's role in denying miracles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four doctrines of the philosophers as presented by Ghazali, and why the first doctrine (necessary causality) leads to the denial of miracles.

Core Idea
The philosophers' necessary causality makes miracles impossible, forcing allegorical interpretation of the Quran.

Ghazali opens his critique by listing the philosophers' four key doctrines. While all four are important, the first—the necessary connection between cause and effect—is the root of their denial of miracles. This passage outlines those doctrines and shows how the first one leads to rejecting the literal occurrence of miracles like reviving the dead.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get an overall sense, then read the translation. Identify the four doctrines as they appear.
Notice the structured listing: the first doctrine is about causality, the second about the soul, etc.
2
Identify each doctrine
For each of the four doctrines, extract the key claim. The first is about necessary conjunction of cause and effect. The second about souls as self-subsistent substances. The third about the soul's eternity. The fourth about no return to bodies.
The Arabic uses 'الأولى', 'الثانية', etc. to mark them.
3
Focus on the first doctrine
Ghazali says the dispute arises only from the first doctrine. Understand why: if cause and effect are necessarily linked, then customary patterns are fixed, making miracles impossible.
This is the central point for the rest of Ghazali's argument.
4
Connect to miracles and interpretation
The philosophers, holding to necessary causality, reinterpret Quranic miracles like reviving the dead as metaphorical. Contrast this with the literal understanding.
This shows the stakes of the debate.

Second: Their saying: Human souls are substances subsisting by themselves, not impressed in the body, and that the meaning of death is the severance of their relation with the body by the severance of governance, otherwise it is subsisting by itself in the state of death, and they claimed that this is known by rational proof. Third: Their saying: These souls—it is impossible for them to perish; rather, when they exist, they are eternal, everlasting, their annihilation is not conceivable. Fourth: Their saying: It is impossible to return these souls to bodies. And the dispute only arises from the first [doctrine], because upon it is built the proof of miracles that break custom, such as turning the staff into a serpent, reviving the dead, and splitting the moon; and whoever makes the customary courses necessarily necessary, he would deem all that impossible, and would interpret what is in the Quran concerning reviving the dead, saying: He intended by it removing the death of ignorance by the life of knowledge.

Commentary
This passage presents the philosophers' four key doctrines and then focuses on the first, the necessitarian view of causality, as the basis for denying miracles. It explains that if causality is necessary, then miracles like reviving the dead are impossible, and they reinterpret Quranic verses allegorically. The passage lists the doctrines of causality, soul as self-subsistent, soul's indestructibility, and no return to bodies, then argues that the first doctrine leads to the denial of miracles.

The Philosophers' Theory of Prophetic Intuition and Miracles
Ghazali outlines the philosophers' theory of prophetic intuition and the soul's influence on nature.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the philosophers explain prophetic knowledge through intuitive power and how the soul can influence both the body and external nature, as expounded by Ghazali.

Core Idea
Prophetic miracles arise from the soul's natural power but are limited to natural possibilities.

Ghazali presents the philosophers' theory of prophetic cognition and miracles with remarkable detail. He explains how people differ in intuitive ability, and how the soul's practical power can affect the body and even external elements. This sets the stage for his critical response.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on degrees of intuition
Read carefully how Ghazali describes the variation in human intuitive ability, from none to prophetic completeness.
Notice the analogy with extremes of deficiency and strength.
2
Understand the example of the plank
The plank example illustrates how bodily reaction follows vivid imagination; grasp its role in the argument.
This is key to the practical psychic power.
3
Identify the limits of prophetic miracles according to the philosophers
Notice that the philosophers admit miracles only within natural causation, excluding transformations like stick to snake.
Ghazali later refutes this limitation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذ ذكر له الدليل تنبه للمدلول من نفسه، وبالجملة إذا خطر له الحد الأوسط تنبه للنتيجة، وإذا حضر في ذهنه حدا النتيجة خطر بباله الحد الأوسط الجامع بين طرفي النتيجة. والناس في هذا منقسمون: فمنهم من يتنبه بنفسه، ومنهم من يتنبه بأدنى تنبيه، ومنهم من لا يدرك مع التنبيه إلا بتعب كثير. وإذا جاز أن ينتهي طرف النقصان إلى من لا حدس له أصلا حتى لا يتهيأ لفهم المعقولات بالتنبيه، جاز أن ينتهي طرف القوة والزيادة إلى أن يتنبه لكل المعقولات أو لأكثرها وفي أسرع الأوقات وأقربها. ويختلف ذلك بالكمية في جميع المطالب أو بعضها، وفي الكيفية حتى يتفاوت في القرب والبعد. فرب نفس مقدسة صافية يستمر حدسها في جميع المعقولات وفي أسرع الأوقات؛ فهو النبي الذي له معجزة من القوة كأنه النظرية، فلا يحتاج في المعقولات إلى معلم بل يتعلم من نفسه، وهو الذي وصف بأنه «يكاد زيتها يضيء ولو لم تمسسه نار، نور على نور». الثالث: القوة النفسية العملية، فقد تنتهي إلى حد تتأثر بها الطبيعيات وتتسخر. ومثاله أن النفس منا متى توهمت شيئاً خدمتها الأعضاء والقوى التي فيها فتحركت إلى الجهة المتخيلة المطلوبة؛ حتى إذا توهمت شيئاً طيب المذاق تحلبت أشداقه، وانتهضت القوة الملعبة فياضة باللعاب من معادنه، وإذا تصور الوقاع انتهضت القوة فنشرت الآلة، بل إذا مشى على جذع ممدود على فضاء طرفاه على حائطين اشتد توهمه للسقوط فانفعل الجسم بتوهمه وسقط؛ ولو كان ذلك على الأرض لمشى عليه ولم يسقط. وذلك لأن الأجسام والقوى الجسمانية خلقت خادمة مسخرة للنفوس، ويختلف ذلك باختلاف صفاء النفوس وقوتها. فلا يبعد أن تبلغ قوة النفس إلى حد تخدمها القوة الطبيعية في غير بدنه، لأن نفسه ليست منطبعة في بدنه إلا أن لها نوع نزوع وشوق إلى تدبيره، خلق ذلك في جبلته. فإذا جاز أن تطيعها أجسام بدنها لم يمتنع أن يطيعها غيرها. فتتطلع نفسه إلى هبوب ريح أو نزول مطر أو هجوم صاعقة أو تزلزل أرض لتخسف بقوم؛ وذلك موقوف حصوله على حدوث برودة أو سخونة أو حركة في الهواء، فيحدث من نفسه تلك السخونة والبرودة ويتولد منها هذه الأمور من غير حضور سبب طبيعي ظاهر، ويكون ذلك معجزة للنبي عليه السلام ولكنه إنما يحصل ذلك في هواء مستعد للقبول، ولا ينتهي إلى أن ينقلب الخشب حيواناً وينفلق القمر الذي لا يقبل الانخراق. فهذا مذهبهم في المعجزات، ونحن لا ننكر شيئاً مما ذكروه وأن ذلك مما يكون للأنبياء صلوات الله عليهم وسلامه، وإنما ننكر اقتصارهم عليه ومنعهم قلب العصا حية وإحياء الموتى وغيره. فلزم الخوض في هذه المسألة لإثبات المعجزات ولأمر آخر وهو نصرة ما أطبق عليه المسلمون من أن الله تعالى قادر على كل شيء، فلنخض في المقصود.
And when the proof is mentioned to him, he becomes alert to the conclusion from himself. In summary, when the middle term occurs to him, he becomes alert to the result. And when the two bounds of the conclusion are present in his mind, the middle term that connects the two sides of the conclusion occurs to him. People are divided in this: some become alert by themselves, some become alert with the slightest prompting, and some do not grasp it even with prompting except with much effort. And if it is possible that the extreme of deficiency ends with one who has no intuition at all, so that he cannot be prepared to understand intelligibles even with prompting, it is possible that the extreme of strength and increase ends with one who becomes alert to all or most intelligibles in the quickest and nearest times. And that varies in quantity, concerning all or some of the objectives, and in quality, so that it varies in nearness and distance. So there may be a pure, holy soul whose intuition continues in all intelligibles and in the quickest times; that is the prophet who has the miracle of theoretical power, so that he does not need a teacher in intelligibles but learns from himself, and he is the one described as 'its oil would almost shine even if no fire touched it, light upon light.' The third: the practical psychic power, which may reach such a degree that natural things are affected by it and become subservient. An example of this: when our soul imagines something, the limbs and the powers within it serve it and move towards the imagined direction sought, so that when it imagines something with a pleasant taste, his mouth waters, and the salivary gland rises overflowing with saliva from its sources. And when he imagines sexual intercourse, the power rises and the organ spreads. Indeed, when someone walks on a plank stretched over a void, its two ends on two walls, his imagination of falling intensifies, and the body is affected by his imagination and he falls; whereas if it were on the ground, he would walk on it and not fall. That is because bodies and bodily powers were created serving and subservient to souls, and that varies according to the purity and strength of souls. It is not far-fetched that the power of the soul should reach such a degree that the natural power serves it in something other than its own body, because the soul is not imprinted in its body, only it has a certain inclination and desire for its management, which was created in its nature. So if it is possible that the bodies of its own body obey it, it is not impossible that other bodies obey it. Then its soul may aspire to a wind blowing, or rain falling, or a lightning bolt striking, or an earthquake to swallow a people; and that depends on the occurrence of coldness or heat or motion in the air, and that coldness and heat arise from that soul, and these things are generated from it without the presence of an apparent natural cause. And that would be a miracle for the prophet, peace be upon him. However, that only happens in air that is prepared to receive it, and does not extend to wood turning into an animal or the moon splitting, which does not admit of being cleft. This is their doctrine concerning miracles. We do not deny anything they mentioned, and that such things do occur for the prophets (peace and blessings of Allah upon them). We only deny their limiting it to that and their preventing the turning of a stick into a snake, the reviving of the dead, and other things. So it became necessary to delve into this question for establishing miracles, and for another purpose: supporting what Muslims have agreed upon, that Allah Almighty is powerful over all things. So let us delve into the intended discussion.
Source pages p. 237

Commentary
This passage is an exposition and refutation. Ghazali first explains the philosophers' theory of prophetic cognition and miracles. He describes how people differ in their intuitive ability (hads): some grasp conclusions immediately, others need prompting, and some struggle. He argues by analogy: just as there is an extreme of deficiency (no intuition), there can be an extreme of strength (prophetic intuition). The prophet possesses a holy soul that intuits all intelligibles instantly, needing no teacher. This is the 'theoretical power' miracle. Then Ghazali presents the philosophers' account of the practical psychic power (al-quwwa al-nafsiyya al-'amaliyya). When the soul imagines something, the body obeys it, as in salivation at imagining tasty food or erection at imagining intercourse. The example of walking on a plank shows how fear of falling causes actual falling. Since bodies naturally serve souls, it is plausible that a strong soul can affect external natural phenomena like wind, rain, earthquakes, by generating the required qualities in the air, provided the air is receptive. The philosophers limit miracles to such natural possibilities and deny transformations like a stick becoming a snake or the moon splitting. Ghazali concludes that while he does not reject these natural miracles, he rejects the philosophers' restriction, affirming that Allah is omnipotent and can do all things, including miracles that transcend natural causality. Thus he sets the stage for his own argument on miracles.

Key Terms
الحدس (hads / intuition)
الحد الأوسط (middle term)
القوة النفسية العملية (practical psychic power)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the philosophers reject all miracles, but they actually affirm prophetic influence within natural limits.
Correction: The passage shows that philosophers accept miracles like wind, rain, and earthquakes as caused by prophetic souls, but they deny supernatural transformations.

Checkpoint
What does the example of the plank illustrate?
A) The soul's imagination can affect bodily movement, causing a fall.
B) Planks are dangerous to walk on.
C) The philosophers reject all miracles.
D) Ghazali denies the soul's influence on the body.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers explain miracles as natural effects of a strong soul, but they deny any miracle that violates natural laws, such as a stick turning into a snake.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The philosophers say the soul can only affect nature within its preparedness. If that is true for the strongest souls, what does that imply about your own spiritual state? Are you preparing your soul to be receptive to divine influence, or are you neglecting its cultivation?
?
The prophet needed no teacher for intelligibles. In what areas of your life are you relying on your own limited intuition instead of seeking guidance from the Qur'an and Sunnah? What concrete step will you take today to align your soul with the prophetic model?

Causality and Created Things
